<TABLE>
<TR><TD COLSPAN=5><a name="description"></a>&nbsp;<p><p align='center'><strong>3.A.3  The P-type ATPase (P-ATPase) Superfamily</strong></p>
<p>Nearly all of the members of this superfamily, found in bacteria, archaea and eukaryotes, catalyze cation uptake and/or efflux driven by ATP hydrolysis.  Clustering on the phylogenetic tree is usually in accordance with specificity for the transported ion(s).  Many of these protein complexes are multisubunit with a large subunit serving the primary ATPase and ion translocation functions.  In eukaryotes, they are present in the plasma membranes or endoplasmic reticular membranes.  In prokaryotes, they are localized to the cytoplasmic membranes.  Gastric H<sup>+</sup>-translocating ATPases (ouabain-insensitive) comprise a subgroup of the larger and more diverse Na<sup>+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup> ATPase family (ouabain-sensitive) (Family 1).  The ouabain-binding pocket can be functionally reconstituted in the gastric H<sup>+</sup>,K<sup>+</sup> ATPase by substitution of only seven residues (<span id='ref3249.16051601' class='cite'>Qiu <em>et al</em>., 2005</span>).  Ca<sup>2+</sup> ATPases of prokaryotes and eukaryotes comprise a very diverse family (Family 2) including in eukaryotes plasma membrane, golgi, and sarcoplasmic reticular types.  Sarcoplasmic reticular Ca<sup>2+</sup> ATPases (SERCA) in brown adipose tissue can uncouple ATP hydrolysis from Ca<sup>2+</sup> transport and be thermogenic (de <span id='ref2617.12912988' class='cite'>Meis, 2003</span>).  H<sup>+</sup>-translocating P-type ATPases of plants and fungi comprise their own family (Family 3). Plant P-ATPases have been reviewed by Wdowikowska and Klobus, (<span id='ref8377.21735823' class='cite'>2011</span>). Distinct bacterial enzymes specific for K<sup>+</sup> (Family 7; only in prokaryotes) or Mg<sup>2+</sup> (Family 4, mostly in prokaryotes; uptake), Cu<sup>2+</sup>, Ag<sup>+</sup>, Zn<sup>2+</sup>, Co<sup>2+</sup>, Pb<sup>2+</sup>, Ni<sup>2+</sup>, and/or Cd<sup>2+</sup> (Family 6; efflux) and Cu<sup>2+</sup> or Cu<sup>+</sup> (Family 5; uptake or efflux, depending on the system) have been characterized, and each of these types of enzymes comprises a distinct family.  Cu<sup>2+</sup> or Cu<sup>+</sup>-translocating ATPases from bacteria, archaea and animals cluster together, and at least some of these also transport Ag<sup>+</sup>. The Cu<sup>+</sup>/Ag<sup>+</sup> (Family 5 and heavy metal (Family 6)) ATPases have an 8 TMS topology (<span id='ref2173.11756450' class='cite'>Mandal <em>et al</em>., 2002</span>).  A cys-pro-cys motif in CopA of <em>E. coli</em> (TC #3.A.3.5.5) is essential for Cu<sup>+</sup>/Ag<sup>+</sup> efflux and phosphoenzyme formation (<span id='ref2398.12351646' class='cite'>Fan and Rosen, 2002</span>). <span id='ref34555202.31874046' class='cite'>Dyla <em>et al.</em> 2020</span> have reviewed the structures, functions, and dynamics of P2-ATPases including Ca<sup>2+</sup>-ATPases
 and the Na,K-ATPases. They highlight mechanisms of functional transitions that
 are associated with ion exchange on either side of the membrane and how
 the functional cycle is regulated by interaction partners, 
autoregulatory domains, and off-cycle states.</p>
<p>P-type ATPases play essential roles in numerous processes, which in 
humans include nerve impulse propagation, relaxation of muscle fibers, 
secretion and absorption in the kidney, acidification of the stomach and
 nutrient absorption in the intestine. Published evidence suggests that 
uncharacterized families of P-type ATPases with novel specificities 
exist. Thever &amp; Saier (<span id='ref7507.19548020' class='cite'>2009</span>) analyzed the fully sequenced genomes of 26 eukaryotes including animals, plants, fungi and unicellular eukaryotes for P-type ATPases. They reported the organismal distributions, 
phylogenetic relationships, probable topologies and conserved motifs of 
nine functionally characterized families and 13 uncharacterized families
 of these enzyme transporters. A similar but more extensive study on Prokaryotic P-type ATPase was presented by&nbsp;<span id='ref7508.20962537' class='cite'>Chan <em>et al.</em> 2010</span> (see below). Family 9 Na<sup>+</sup>- or K<sup>+</sup>-ATPases can be found in fungi, plants bryophytes and protozoa (<span id='ref7523.20650263' class='cite'>Rodr&iacute;guez-Navarro and Benito, 2010</span>). The Na<sup>+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase acts as a receptor for cardiotonic steroids such as ouabain. Cardiotonic steroids inhibit this enzyme's activity, resulting in modulation of Ca<sup>2+</sup> levels, which are essential for homeostasis in neurons. Two points regarding the interplay between the Na<sup>+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase and Ca<sup>2+</sup> signalling in the brain are (1) Na<sup>+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase impairment causes illness and neuronal death due to deficient Ca<sup>2+</sup> signalling and (2) benefits to the brain result from modulating Na<sup>+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase activity. These interactions play an essential role in neuronal cell fate determination (<span id='ref34554812.33644859' class='cite'>Kinoshita <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). The significance of the plasma membrane H<sup>+</sup>-ATPase and the V-ATPase for growth and pathogenicity in pathogenic fungi has been discussed (<span id='ref34557043.37597947' class='cite'>Yang and Peng 2023</span>).</p>
<p>Chan et al. (<span id='ref7508.20962537' class='cite'>2010</span>) analyzed P-type ATPases in all major prokaryotic phyla for which complete
 genome sequence data were available and compared the results with 
those for eukaryotic P-type ATPases. Topological type I (heavy metal) 
P-type ATPases predominate in prokaryotes (approx. tenfold) while type 
II ATPases (specific for Na<sup>+</sup>,K<sup>+</sup>, H<sup>+</sup> Ca<sup>2+</sup>, Mg<sup>2+</sup> and 
phospholipids) predominate in eukaryotes (approx. twofold). Many P-type 
ATPase families are found exclusively in prokaryotes (e.g. Kdp-type K<sup>+</sup> uptake ATPases (type III) and all ten prokaryotic functionally uncharacterized P-type ATPase (FUPA) familes), while 
others are restricted to eukaryotes (e.g. phospholipid flippases and all
 13 eukaryotic FUPA families) (<span id='ref7507.19548020' class='cite'>Thever and Saier, 2009</span>). Horizontal gene transfer has occurred 
frequently among bacteria and archaea, which have similar distributions 
of these enzymes, but rarely between most eukaryotic kingdoms, and even 
more rarely between eukaryotes and prokaryotes. In some bacterial phyla 
(e.g. Bacteroidetes, Flavobacteria and Fusobacteria), ATPase gene gain 
and loss as well as horizontal transfer occurred seldom in contrast to 
most other bacterial phyla. Some families (i.e., Kdp-type ATPases) 
underwent far less horizontal gene transfer than other prokaryotic 
families, possibly due to their multisubunit characteristics. Functional
 motifs are better conserved across family lines than across organismal 
lines, and these motifs can be family specific, facilitating functional 
predictions. In some cases, gene fusion events created P-type ATPases 
covalently linked to regulatory catalytic enzymes. In one family (FUPA 
Family 24), a type I ATPase gene (N-terminal) is fused to a type II 
ATPase gene (C-terminal) with retention of function only for the latter.
 Several pseudogene-encoded nonfunctional ATPases were identified. 
Genome minimalization led to preferential loss of P-type ATPase genes. Chan et al. (<span id='ref7508.20962537' class='cite'>2010</span>) suggested that in prokaryotes and some unicellular eukaryotes, the 
primary function of P-type ATPases is protection from extreme 
environmental stress conditions. The classification of P-type ATPases of
 unknown function into phylogenetic families provides guides for future 
molecular biological studies (<span id='ref7508.20962537' class='cite'>Chan <em>et al.</em>, 2010</span>).</p>
<p>Many eukaryotic P-type ATPases are monomeric or homodimeric enzymes of 
the catalytic subunit that hydrolyzes ATP. They contain the aspartyl 
phosphorylation site and catalyzes ion transport. The Na<sup>+</sup>,K<sup>+</sup>-ATPases,
 the Ca<sup>2+</sup>-ATPases and the (fungal) H<sup>+</sup>-ATPases of 
higher organisms exhibit 10 transmembrane &alpha; helical spanners 
(TMSs), some of them highly tilted. Additional subunits that appear to 
lack catalytic activity may be present in the ATPase complex. For 
example, the 10 TMS catalytic &alpha;-subunit of the Na<sup>+</sup>,K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase
 of animals is tightly complexed to the 1 TMS &beta;-subunit and the 
tissue-specific, regulatory, 1 TMS &gamma;-subunit. The &beta;-subunit, which may 
influence the activity of the &alpha;-subunit, probably functions to 
facilitate proper insertion of the &alpha;-subunit into the membrane, to allow
 proper targeting to a subcellular membrane site in post-translational 
processing, and to stabilize the catalytic subunit. The &beta;-subunit can 
therefore be considered to be an auxiliary protein of the Na<sup>+</sup>,K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase
 catalytic subunit.  The &gamma;-subunit of the Na<sup>+</sup>,K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase
 has been reported to influence kinetic parameters and is homologous to a
 family of pore-forming peptides, the peptides of the phospholemman 
family (TC #1.A.27), and the C-subunits of V-type ATPases (TC #3.A.2). 
This &gamma;-subunit is induced under stress conditions and modulates Na<sup>+</sup>,K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase
 activity and cell growth (<span id='ref3031.15280368' class='cite'>Wetzel
 <em>et al</em>., 2004</span>).  The Na<sup>+</sup>, K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase 
can serve as a steroid hormone receptor (<span id='ref2175.12027881' class='cite'>Schoner, 2002</span>). Several other P-type ATPases also 
depend on small proteolipids, the functions of which are uncertain.</p>
<p>The annular lipid-protein stoichiometry in a native pig kidney Na<sup>+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase 
preparation has been studied by [125I]TID-PC/16 labeling, giving results that indicated 
that the transmembrane domain of the Na<sup>+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase in the E1 state is 
less exposed to the lipids than in the E2 state, i.e., the conformational 
transitions are accompanied by changes in the numbers of annular lipids 
but not in the affinity of these lipids for the protein (<span id='ref8914.21419126' class='cite'>Mangialavori <em>et al.</em> 2011</span>). The 
lipid-protein stoichiometry was 23 &plusmn; 2 (&alpha; subunit) and 5.0 &plusmn; 0.4 (&beta; 
subunit) in the E1 conformation and 32 &plusmn; 2 (&alpha; subunit) and 7 &plusmn; 1 (&beta; 
subunit) in the E2 conformation. <span id='ref34556892.37478126' class='cite'>Ahmad <em>et al.</em> 2023 </span>provided an overview of current approaches to developing stimuli-responsive ion transport systems, including channels and mobile carriers, that can be controlled using photo or redox inputs.</p>
<p>The stoichiometries of transport are sometimes known and complex.  In the case of the Na<sup>+</sup>,K<sup>+</sup>-ATPases, 3 Na<sup>+</sup><span> are exchanged for 2 K<sup>+</sup></span> per ATP molecule hydrolyzed.  The gastric H<sup>+</sup>-translocating ATPases replace H<sup>+</sup> for K<sup>+</sup> but with an H<sup>+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup> stoichiometry of 2:2.  Thus, although these two enzymes are ~65% identical, the Na<sup>+</sup>,K<sup>+</sup>-ATPases are electrogenic while the H<sup>+</sup>,K<sup>+</sup>-ATPases are electroneutral. Gastric H<sup>+</sup>, K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase transports 2 moles of H<sup>+</sup> together with two H<sub>2</sub>O (two H<sub>3</sub>O<sup>+</sup>) per mole of ATP hydrolyzed in isolated hog gastric vesicles.  Protons are charge-transferred from the cytosolic side to H<sub>2</sub>O in sites 2 and 1, the H<sub>2</sub>O coming from the cytosol, and H<sub>3</sub>O<sup>+</sup> in these sites are transported into the lumen during the conformational transition from E<sub>1</sub>P to E<sub>2</sub>P (<span id='ref5661.18403373' class='cite'>Morii <em>et al</em>., 2008</span>).  Ca<sup>2+</sup> ATPases may catalyze Ca<sup>2+</sup>/K<sup>+ </sup>or Ca<sup>2+</sup>/H<sup>+</sup> antiport.  A single organism often possesses multiple isoforms of these enzymes. Caloxin-derived peptides in the micromolar range are inhibitors of plasma membrane calcium ATPases (<span id='ref34554892.35605801' class='cite'>Boutin <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>).</p>
<p>Considerable evidence is available showing that animals have Cl<sup>-</sup> translocating, Cl<sup>-</sup> stimulating P-type ATPases.  Although extensive biochemical data are available, the protein sequence of any one such Cl<sup>-</sup> ATPase has not yet been determined (<span id='ref1417.8250876' class='cite'>Gerencser, 1993</span>; <span id='ref1419.8759922' class='cite'>Inagaki <em>et al</em>., 1996</span>; <span id='ref1436.9875533' class='cite'>Zeng <em>et al</em>., 1999</span>). Evidence for mammalian iron-inducible, iron-transporting ATPases is also available (<span id='ref1437.10809751' class='cite'>Baranano <em>et al</em>., 2000</span>). Finally bacterial Na<sup>+</sup>-transporting P-type ATPases probably exist (<span id='ref1445.10799538' class='cite'>Ueno <em>et al</em>., 2000</span>).  Evidence for a Na<sup>+</sup>-P-type ATPase has been obtained for the halotolerant cyanobacterium, <em>Aphanothece halophytica </em>(<span id='ref5450.17302934' class='cite'>Wiangon <em>et al</em>., 2007</span>). Thus the breadth of substrates transported by P-type ATPases is likely to be much greater than currently recognized.</p>
<p>The Na<sup>+</sup>,K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase acts as a signal transducer and transcription activator, modulating cell growth, apoptosis, and cell motility. A prominent binding motif linking the Na<sup>+</sup>,K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase to intracellular signaling effectors is the N-terminal tail of the Na<sup>+</sup>,K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase catalytic &alpha;-subunit which binds directly to the N-terminus of the inositol 1,4,5-trisphosphate receptor (<span id='ref3787.16723354' class='cite'>Zhang <em>et al</em>., 2006</span>). Three amino acyl residues, LKK, conserved in most species and most &alpha;-isoforms, are essential for binding. In wild-type cells, low concentrations of ouabain trigger low frequency calcium oscillations that activate NF-&kappa;B and protect from apoptosis. Thus, the LKK motif binds the inositol 1,4,5-trisphosphate receptor and triggers an anti-apoptotic calcium signal. However, the N-terminal hydrophilic region in front of the first TMS does not interact with the transported cation (Na<sup>+</sup>, K<sup>+</sup>, or Ca<sup>2+</sup>) although this first TMS does (<span id='ref4552.' class='cite'>Einholm <em>et al</em>., 2007</span>). Of the P-type ATPases, only Na<sup>+</sup>, K<sup>+</sup>-ATPases are receptors that respond to endogenous cardiotonic steroids such as ouabain and marinobufagenin (steroid 'hormones') which regulate Na<sup>+</sup> excretion and blood pressure (<span id='ref6876.20144708' class='cite'>Liu and Xie, 2010</span>).</p>
<p>P-type ATPases provide a polar transmembrane pathway, to which access is strictly controlled by coupled gates that are constrained to open alternately, thereby enabling thermodynamically uphill ion transport. Reyes and Gadsby (<span id='ref4992.17006516' class='cite'>2006</span>) have examined the ion pathway through the N<sup>+</sup>, K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase, a representative P-type pump, after uncoupling its extra- and intracellular gates with the marine toxin palytoxin. They found a wide outer vestibule penetrating deep into the Na<sup>+</sup>, K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase, where the pathway narrows and leads to a charge-selectivity filter. Acidic residues in this region, which are conserved to coordinate pumped ions, allow the approach of cations but exclude anions. Reversing the charge at just one of those positions converts the pathway from cation selective to anion selective.  Cysteine scans from TM1 to TM6 in the Na<sup>+</sup>, K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase revealed a single unbroken cation pathway  that traverses palytoxin-bound Na<sup>+</sup>,K<sup>+</sup>-pump-channels from one side  of the membrane to the other (<span id='ref6156.18849964' class='cite'>Takeuchi <em>et al.</em> 2008</span>). This pathway comprises residues from TM1,  TM2, TM4 and TM6, passes through ion-binding site II, and is probably  conserved in structurally and evolutionarily related P-type pumps.  Close structural homology among the catalytic subunits of Ca<sup>2+</sup>-, Na<sup>+</sup>, K<sup>+</sup>- and H<sup>+</sup>, K<sup>+</sup>-ATPases argues that their extracytosolic cation exchange pathways all share these physical characteristics (<span id='ref4992.17006516' class='cite'>Reyes and Gadsby, 2006</span>). The mechanistic details of type II ATPases, notably those for which 3-d structures are available (Na, K<sup>+</sup>-, gastri H<sup>+</sup>, K<sup>+</sup>-, Ca<sup>2+</sup> and H<sup>+</sup>, K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase); TC Families 1,2 and 3, respectively, as well as prepared cation translocation pathways, have been discussed by Bublitz et al. (<span id='ref7407.20634056' class='cite'>2010</span>). P-type ATPase in several Rosaceae species including the pear have been identified (<span id='ref34551029.32057915' class='cite'>Zhang <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>).</p>
<p>The X-ray crystal structure at 3.5 &Aring; resolution of the pig renal Na<sup>+</sup>,K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase has been determined with two rubidium ions bound in an occluded state in the transmembrane part of the &alpha;-subunit (<span id='ref4783.18075585' class='cite'>Morth <em>et al</em>., 2007</span>). Several of the residues forming the cavity for rubidium/potassium occlusion in the Na<sup>+</sup>,K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase are homologous to those binding calcium in the Ca<sup>2+</sup>-ATPase of the sarco(endo)plasmic reticulum. The &beta;- and &gamma;-subunits specific to the Na<sup>+</sup>,K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase are associated with transmembrane helices &alpha;M7/&alpha;M10 and &alpha;M9, respectively. The &gamma;-subunit corresponds to a fragment of the V-type ATPase c subunit. The carboxy terminus of the &alpha;-subunit is contained within a pocket between transmembrane helices and seems to be a novel regulatory element controlling sodium affinity, possibly influenced by the membrane potential.</p>
<p>The Na<sup>+</sup>,K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase can be transformed into an ion channel using pharmacological agents. Palytoxin (PTX), produced by soft coral of the genus <em>Palythoa,</em> binds to the ATPase with a Kd of 20 pM and creates a monovalent cation-selective channel with a single channel conductance of 10 pS (<span id='ref7691.20932855' class='cite'>Rossini and Bigiani, 2011</span>). The presence of external Na<sup>+</sup> seems to be essential for channel activation (<span id='ref2419.12507758' class='cite'>Wu <em>et al</em>., 2003</span>). When the N-terminal 35 residues are removed from the ATPase, the toxin-activated channel does not exhibit a time-dependent inactivation gating at positive potentials as is characteristic of the wild-type protein. The truncated pump exhibits no electrogenic current, and the ion stoichiometry for active transport is altered. Addition of the synthetic peptide restores activity towards wild type. The N-terminal peptide therefore appears to act as an inactivation gate (similar to Shaker B channels of the VIC family (TC #1.A.1)). It may also play a critical role in determining the ion stoichiometry (<span id='ref2419.12507758' class='cite'>Wu <em>et al</em>., 2003</span>). Fluorometric studies indicate that under normal conditions, &alpha;- and &beta;-subunits move towards each other during the E<sub>2</sub> to E<sub>1</sub> transition (<span id='ref3785.16980302' class='cite'>Dempski <em>et al</em>., 2006</span>).</p>
<p>The structures of the sarcoplasmic reticular Ca<sup>2+</sup>-ATPase have been solved at 2.6 &Aring; resolution for the complex to which 2 Ca<sup>2+</sup> are bound, and at 3.1 &Aring; resolution for the complex lacking Ca<sup>2+</sup> (<span id='ref2247.10864315' class='cite'>Toyoshima <em>et al</em>., 2000</span>; <span id='ref2248.12167852' class='cite'>Toyoshima and Nomura, 2002</span>).  A total of eight different states of the Ca<sup>2+</sup> -ATPase, representing the many steps in the reaction cycle, have been visualized by high resolution x-ray crystallography<em> </em>(<span id='ref7179.18077416' class='cite'>Toyoshima <em>et al.</em>, 2007</span>; <span id='ref7180.18455499' class='cite'>Toyoshima, 2008</span>). The two Ca<sup>2+</sup> are located side by side, surrounded by 4 transmembrane helices, two of which are unwound for efficient coordination geometry. There are 3 cytoplasmic domains, one, the central catalytic domain, bearing the phosphorylation site, a second bearing the adenosine nucleotide binding site, and a third of unknown function. The central domain has the same fold as haloacid dehydrogenases (<span id='ref2085.9584613' class='cite'>Aravind <em>et al</em>., 1998</span>; <span id='ref2246.10733958' class='cite'>Stokes and Green, 2000</span>). The Ca<sup>2+</sup>-free form shows large conformational differences from the Ca<sup>2+</sup>-bound form with the three cytoplasmic domains tightly associated to form a single headpiece and six of the ten TMSs largely rearranged. These latter rearrangements guarantee the release of external Ca<sup>2+</sup> and create a pathway for entry of Ca<sup>2+</sup> from the cytoplasm.  ATPase activity and Ca<sup>2+</sup> binding are cooperatively interdependent, but the two processes can be separated by mutations (Zhang et al., 2002).</p>
<p>Structures are available for both the E1 and E2 states of the Ca<sup>2+</sup> ATPase showing that Ca<sup>2+</sup> binding induces major changes in all three cytoplasmic domains relative to each other (<span id='ref2249.11829513' class='cite'>Xu <em>et al</em>., 2002</span>). Xu et al. proposed how Ca<sup>2+</sup> binding induces conformational changes in TMS4 and 5 in the membrane domain (M) that in turn induce rotation of the phosphorylation domain (P). The nucleotide binding (N) and &beta;-sheet (&beta;) domains are highly mobile, with N flexibly linked to P, and &beta; flexibly linked to M. Modeling of the fungal H<sup>+</sup> ATPase, based on the structures of the Ca<sup>2+</sup> pump, suggested a comparable 70&ordm; rotation of N relative to P to deliver ATP to the phosphorylation site (<span id='ref2244.12169656' class='cite'>K&uuml;hlbrandt <em>et al</em>., 2002</span>). One report suggests that this S.R. Ca<sup>2+</sup> ATPase is homodimeric (<span id='ref5296.18471093' class='cite'>Ushimaru and Fukushima, 2008</span>).</p>
<p>Crystal structures (Gadsby, 2007) have shown that the conserved TGES loop of the Ca<sup>2+</sup>-ATPase is isolated in the Ca<sub>2</sub><em>E</em>1 state but becomes inserted in the catalytic site in <em>E</em>2 states. Anthonisen et al. (<span id='ref3649.16893884' class='cite'>2006</span>) characterized the kinetics of the partial reaction steps of the transport cycle and the binding of the phosphoryl analogs BeF, AlF, MgF, and vanadate in mutants with alterations to the TGES residues. The data provide functional evidence supporting a role of Glu<sup>183</sup> in activating the water molecule involved in the <em>E</em>2P &rarr; <em>E</em>2 dephosphorylation and suggest a direct participation of the side chains of the TGES loop in the control and facilitation of the insertion of the loop in the catalytic site. The interactions of the TGES loop furthermore seem to facilitate its disengagement from the catalytic site during the <em>E</em>2 &rarr; Ca<sub>2</sub><em>E</em>1 transition.</p>
<p>Olesen et al. (<span id='ref4782.18075584' class='cite'>2007</span>) have described functional studies and three new crystal structures of the rabbit skeletal muscle Ca<sup>2+</sup>-ATPase. These represent the phosphoenzyme intermediates associated with (1) Ca<sup>2+</sup> binding, (2) Ca<sup>2+</sup> translocation and (3) dephosphorylation. They are based on complexes with a functional ATP analogue, beryllium fluoride or aluminium fluoride. The structures complete the cycle of nucleotide binding and cation transport of Ca<sup>2+</sup>-ATPase. Phosphorylation of the enzyme triggers a conformational change that leads to opening of a luminal exit pathway defined by the transmembrane segments M1 through M6. M1-M6 represent the canonical membrane domain of P-type pumps. Ca<sup>2+ </sup>release is promoted by translocation of the M4 helix, exposing Glu 309, Glu 771 and Asn 796 to the lumen. The mechanism explains how P-type ATPases are able to form the steep electrochemical gradients required for key functions in eukaryotic cells (<span id='ref4782.18075584' class='cite'>Olesen <em>et al</em>., 2007</span>). Moller et al. (<span id='ref7511.20665264' class='cite'>2010</span>) reveiwed structural studies of various conformers of the Ca<sup>2+</sup> ATPase, (SERCA1a), present in skeletal muscle. The structures corresponding to 
the various intermediary states. They have been obtained after stabilization 
with structural analogues of ATP or metal fluorides as mimicks of 
inorganic phosphate. It is possible to provide a detailed
 structural description of both ATP hydrolysis and Ca<sup>2+</sup> transport 
through the membrane.</p>
<p>The structure of a P-type proton pump was determined by X-ray crystallography by Pederson et al., (2007). Ten transmembrane helices and three cytoplasmic domains define the functional unit of ATP-coupled proton transport across the plasma membrane. The structure is locked in a functional state not previously observed in P-type ATPases. The transmembrane domain reveals a large cavity, which is likely to be filled with water, located near the middle of the membrane plane where it is lined by conserved hydrophilic and charged residues. Proton transport against a high membrane potential is readily explained by this structural arrangement. The plasma membrane H<sup>+</sup>-ATPase, SmPHA4, negatively regulates the biosynthesis of tanshinones in <em>Salvia miltiorrhiza</em>.(<span id='ref34553444.33805926' class='cite'>Li <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>).&nbsp;<span id='ref34557521.37782798' class='cite'>Galarza-Mu&ntilde;oz <em>et al.</em> 2023</span> identified 12 positions in the Antarctic Na<sup>+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase that, when transferred to a temperate ortholog, were sufficient to confer cold tolerance. Although all 12 Antarctic mutations were required for the full phenotype, a single leucine in the third TMS imparted most of it. Mutations that confer cold resistance are mostly in transmembrane segments, at positions that face the lipid bilayer (<span id='ref34557521.37782798' class='cite'>Galarza-Mu&ntilde;oz <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).</p>
<p>Detailed high resolution x-ray structures of heavy metal P1B-type ATPases (HMAs) were not available prior to 2011 when Gourdon et al. (<span id='ref7542.21716286' class='cite'>2011</span>) reported the structure of CopA, a Cu<sup>+</sup>-ATPase from <em>Legionella pneumophile</em> at 3.2 &Aring; resolution. The results provided the first in depth 
description of a heavy metal-translocatin P1B-type ATPase. A three-stage
 copper transport pathway involves several well conserved residues. A 
P1B-specific transmembrane helix kinks at a double-glycine motif 
displaying an amphipathic helix that lines a putative copper entry point
 at the intracellular interface. An ATPase-coupled copper release 
mechanism from the binding sites in the membrane via an extracellular 
exit site is probable (<span id='ref7542.21716286' class='cite'>Gourdon <em>et al.</em> 2011</span>).&nbsp;<span id='ref34556548.37160901' class='cite'>Batool <em>et al.</em> 2023</span> reported HMAs in eleven different Poaceae species, including wheat (<em>Triticum aestivum)</em> which had&nbsp; 27 HMAs clustering into four distinct clades. TaHMA7, TaHMA8, and TaHMA9 were upregulated in response to elevated copper, suggesting a role in wheat copper homeostasis. Zn<sup>2+</sup>-efflux pumps are electrogenic uniporters that preserve the transport mechanism with 1st-, 2nd- and 3rd-row transition metal substrates (<span id='ref34556726.37293658' class='cite'>Abeyrathna <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). Promiscuous coordination plasticity, guarantees diverse, yet defined, cargo selectivity coupled to their translocation.</p>
<p>As in other P-type ATPases, metal binding to transmembrane metal-binding sites (TM-MBS) in Cu<sup>+</sup>-ATPases is required for enzyme phosphorylation and subsequent transport. However, Cu<sup>+</sup> does not access Cu<sup>+</sup>-ATPases in a free (hydrated) form but is bound to a chaperone protein. The delivery of Cu<sup>+</sup> by <em>Archaeoglobus fulgidus</em> Cu<sup>+</sup> chaperone CopZ to the corresponding Cu<sup>+</sup>-ATPase, CopA, has been studied (<span id='ref5110.18417453' class='cite'>Gonz&aacute;lez-Guerrero and Arg&uuml;ello, 2008</span>). CopZ interacted with and delivered the metal to the N-terminal metal binding domain(s) of CopA (MBDs). Cu<sup>+</sup>-loaded MBDs, acting as metal donors, were unable to activate CopA or a truncated CopA lacking MBDs. Conversely, Cu<sup>+</sup>-loaded CopZ activated the CopA ATPase and CopA constructs in which MBDs were rendered unable to bind Cu<sup>+</sup>. Furthermore, under nonturnover conditions, CopZ transferred Cu<sup>+</sup> to the TM-MBS of a CopA lacking MBDs altogether. Thus, MBDs may serve a regulatory function without participating directly in metal transport, and the chaperone delivers Cu<sup>+</sup> directly to transmembrane transport sites of Cu<sup>+</sup>-ATPases (<span id='ref5110.18417453' class='cite'>Gonz&aacute;lez-Guerrero and Arg&uuml;ello, 2008</span>). Wu et al (<span id='ref5397.18547529' class='cite'>2008</span>) have determined structures of two constructs of the Cu (CopA) pump from <em>Archaeoglobus fulgidus</em> by cryoelectron microscopy of tubular crystals, which revealed the overall architecture and domain organization of the molecule. They localized its N-terminal MBD within the cytoplasmic domains that use ATP hydrolysis to drive the transport cycle and built a pseudoatomic model by fitting existing crystallographic structures into the cryoelectron microscopy maps for CopA. The results also similiarly suggested a Cu-dependent regulatory role for the MBD.</p>
<p>In the <em>Archaeoglobus fulgidus</em> CopA (TC# 3.A.3.5.7), invariant residues in helixes 6, 7 and 8 form two transmembrane metal 
binding sites (TM-MBSs). These bind Cu<sup>+</sup> with high affinity in a 
trigonal planar geometry. The cytoplasmic Cu<sup>+</sup> chaperone CopZ transfers
 the metal directly to the TM-MBSs; however, loading both of the TM-MBSs
 requires binding of nucleotides to the enzyme. In agreement with the 
classical transport mechanism of P-type ATPases, occupancy of both 
transmembrane sites by cytoplasmic Cu<sup>+</sup> is a requirement for enzyme 
phosphorylation and subsequent transport into the periplasmic or 
extracellular milieu. Transport studies have shown that most 
Cu<sup>+</sup>-ATPases drive cytoplasmic Cu<sup>+</sup> efflux, albeit with quite 
different transport rates in tune with their various physiological 
roles. Archetypical Cu<sup>+</sup>-efflux pumps responsible for Cu<sup>+</sup> tolerance, 
like the <em>Escherichia coli</em> CopA, have turnover rates ten times higher 
than those involved in cuproprotein assembly (or alternative functions).
 This explains the incapability of the latter group to significantly 
contribute to the metal efflux required for survival in high copper 
environments.&nbsp; Structural and mechanistic details of
copper-transporting P-type ATPase functionhave been described (<span id='ref34544842.26196187' class='cite'>Meng <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>).</p>
<p>Chintalapati et al. (<span id='ref5123.18374940' class='cite'>2008</span>) have characterized two copper-transporting ATPases, CtrA2 and CtrA3 from <em>Aquifex aeolicus</em>. CtrA2 has a CPC metal-binding sequence in TM6 and a CxxC metal-binding N-terminal domain, while CtrA3 has a CPH metal-binding motif in TM6 and a histidine-rich N-terminal metal-binding domain. CtrA2 is activated by Ag<sup>+</sup> and Cu<sup>+</sup> and presumably transports reduced Cu<sup>+</sup>, while CtrA3 is activated by, and presumably transports, the oxidized copper ion. Both CtrA2 and CtrA3 are thermophilic proteins with an activity maximum at 75 degrees C. Electron cryomicroscopy of two-dimensional crystals of CtrA3 yielded a projection map at approximately 7 A resolution with density peaks indicating eight membrane-spanning alpha-helices per monomer. A fit of the Ca-ATPase structure to the projection map indicates that the arrangement of the six central helices surrounding the ion-binding site in the membrane is conserved, and suggests the position of the two additional N-terminal transmembrane helices that are characteristic of the heavy metal, eight-helix P(1B)-type ATPases (<span id='ref5123.18374940' class='cite'>Chintalapati <em>et al</em>., 2008</span>).&nbsp;</p>
<p>Transmembrane helices contain a cation-binding cysteine-proline-cysteine/histidine/serine (CPx)
motif for catalytic activation and cation translocation. In addition, most Cu-ATPases possess the
N-terminal Cu-binding CxxC motif required for regulation of enzyme activity. In cells, the Cu-
ATPases receive copper from soluble chaperones and maintain intracellular copper homeostasis by
efflux of copper from the cell or transport of the metal into the intracellular compartments (<span id='ref34544578.26422816' class='cite'>Migocka 2015</span>).</p>
<p>The 8TMS CadA of <em>Listeria monocytogenes</em> (Family 6) confirs resistance to cadmium.  Residues in TMS6 (Cys<sup>354</sup> and Cys<sup>356</sup>), TMS8 (Asp<sup>692</sup>) and TMS3 (Met<sup>149</sup>) may bind Cd<sup>2+</sup> (<span id='ref3786.16835223' class='cite'>Wu <em>et al</em>., 2006</span>).  However, the two cysteine residues in the CPC motif act at different steps: Cys<sup>354</sup> is involved in Cd<sup>2+</sup> binding while Cys<sup>356</sup> is involved in Cd<sup>2+</sup> occlusion.  The two equivalent cysteines in the yeast Cu<sup>2+</sup> ATPase may also act at different steps.  The conserved Glu<sup>164</sup> may be required for Cd<sup>2+</sup> release.  Possibly two Cd<sup>2+</sup> are involved in the reaction cycle of CadA (<span id='ref3786.16835223' class='cite'>Wu <em>et al</em>., 2006</span>).&nbsp; A hemerythrin-like two iron-binding domain in a P1B-type transport ATPase from <em>Acidothermus cellulolyticus </em>has been identified (<span id='ref7332.20672819' class='cite'>Traverso <em>et al.</em>, 2010</span>).<em><br /></em></p>
<p>The phospholipid translocating P-type ATPases (Family 8; TC #3.A.3.8) are found only in eukaryotes.  They appear to function with &beta;-subunits of about 400 aas with 2 TMSs.  These have been functionally characterized from yeast and protozoans (TC #8.A.27; <span id='ref3551.16785229' class='cite'>Perez-Victoria <em>et al</em>., 2006</span>). These enzymes catalyze the ATP-dependent flipping of phospholipids and lysophospholipids from the outer leaflet of the cytoplasmic membrane to the inner leaflet. Residues defining phospholipid flippase substrate specificity have been identified (<span id='ref8448.22308393' class='cite'>Baldridge and Graham, 2012</span>). In the yeast <em>Saccharomyces cerevisiae</em>, Family 3.A.3.8 lipid flipping ATPases play a pivotal role in the biogenesis of intracellular transport vesicles, polarized protein transport and protein maturation.&nbsp; However, in mammals, these ATPases act in concert with members of the CDC50 protein family, putative beta-subunits for these ATPases, and many function as part of the vesicle-generating machinery (<span id='ref7092.20450914' class='cite'>Paulusma and Oude Elferink, 2010</span>). Family 8 ATPases may exert their cellular functions by combining enzymatic phospholipid translocation activity with an enzyme-independent action. The latter can involve the timely recruitment of proteins involved in cellular signalling, vesicle coat assembly and cytoskeleton regulation (<span id='ref7765.20836764' class='cite'>van der Velden <em>et al.</em>, 2010</span>).&nbsp;The beta-subunit, CDC50A, allows the stable expression, assembly, subcellular localization, and lipid transport activity of the P4-ATPase ATP8A2 (<span id='ref8659.21454556' class='cite'>Coleman and Molday, 2011</span>).&nbsp; Residues in phospholipid specificity have been identified (<span id='ref8448.22308393' class='cite'>Baldridge and Graham 2012</span>).&nbsp;</p>
<p>Asymmetric phosopholipid distribution in the plasma membranes of animals is disrupted during apoptosis, exposing 
phosphatidylserine (PtdSer) on the cell surface.&nbsp; ATP11C (adenosine triphosphatase 
type 11C) and CDC50A (cell division cycle protein 50A) are required for 
aminophospholipid translocation from the outer to the inner plasma 
membrane leaflet due to their&nbsp; flippase activity. ATP11C 
contain caspase recognition sites, and mutations at these sites 
generate caspase-resistant ATP11C without affecting its flippase 
activity. Cells expressing caspase-resistant ATP11C do not expose 
PtdSer during apoptosis and are not engulfed by macrophages, 
suggesting that inactivation of the flippase activity is required for 
apoptotic PtdSer exposure. CDC50A-deficient cells displayed PtdSer on 
their surface and were engulfed by macrophages, indicating that PtdSer 
is sufficient as an 'eat me' signal.&nbsp; CDC50A serves as a chaparone protein for most phospholipid-flipping ATPases, targetting them (including ATP11C) to the plasma membrane (<span id='ref11192.24904167' class='cite'>Segawa <em>et al.</em> 2014</span>).</p>
<p>P4-ATPases mediate the translocation of phospholipids from the outer 
to the inner leaflet and maintain lipid asymmetry, which is critical for
 membrane trafficking and signaling pathways.&nbsp;<span id='ref34550546.31416931' class='cite'>Hiraizumi <em>et al.</em> 2019</span> reported 
cryo-EM structures of six distinct intermediates of the
 human ATP8A1-CDC50a heterocomplex at resolutions of 2.6 to 3.3 
angstroms, elucidating the lipid translocation cycle of this P4-ATPase. 
ATP-dependent phosphorylation induces a large rotational movement of the
 actuator domain around the phosphorylation site in the phosphorylation 
domain, accompanied by lateral shifts of the first and second 
transmembrane helices, thereby allowing phosphatidylserine binding. The 
phospholipid head group passes through the hydrophilic cleft, while the 
acyl chain is exposed toward the lipid environment (<span id='ref34550546.31416931' class='cite'>Hiraizumi <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>).</p>
<p>An interaction network of the plasma membrane H<sup>+</sup>-ATPase in <em>Bangia fuscopurpurea</em> (Rhodophyta; an economically important intertidal seaweed with a strong tolerance to hypo-salinity. The expression of <em>B. fuscopurpurea</em> plasma membrane H<sup>+</sup>-ATPase (BfPMHA) genes were the most upregulated under hypo-salinity.&nbsp;<span id='ref34556347.37108805' class='cite'>Yao <em>et al.</em> 2023</span> traced the relative expression of this BfPMHA gene in <em>B. fuscopurpurea</em> under hypo-salinity conditions. The expression of BfPMHA increased, and the higher the degree of low salinity stress, the higher the expression level. This BfPMHA had typical PMHA structures with a cation-N domain, an E1-E2 ATPase domain, a hydrolase domain, and seven TMSs. Three proteins interacting with BfPMHA during hypo-saline stress: fructose-bisphosphate aldolase (BfFBA), glyceraldehyde 3-phosphate dehydrogenase (NADP<sup>+</sup>) (phosphorylating) (BfGAPDH), and manganese superoxide dismutase (BfMnSOD). These four genes were transferred and overexpressed in a BY4741 yeast strain which enhanced the yeast tolerance to NaCl stress, verifying the function of BfPMHA in the salt stress response (<span id='ref34556347.37108805' class='cite'>Yao <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).&nbsp;</p>
<p>All P-type ATPases share a common topology with a transmembrane and three cytoplasmic domains, and their transport cycle follows a general scheme - the Post-Albers-cycle. P-type ATPase research has been advanced cryo-EM analyses, which has elucidated many new P-type ATPase structures and mechanisms and revealed several new ways of regulation. <span id='ref34556647.37264943' class='cite'>Stock <em>et al.</em> 2023</span> focused on special features that are present in the five subfamilies. These include (1) the new intersubunit transport model of KdpFABC, (2) the ways in which heavy metal pumps have evolved to accommodate various substrates, (3) the strategies Ca<sup>2+</sup> pumps utilize to adapt to different environmental needs, (4) the intricate molecular builds of the ion binding sites in Na,K- and H,K-ATPases, (6) the remarkable hexameric assembly of fungal proton pumps, (7) the many ways by which P4-ATPase lipid flippases are regulated, and finally (8) the deorphanization of P5 pumps. Interestingly many of the described features are found in more than one of the five subfamilies, and they are mixed and matched together to provide optimal function and precise regulation (<span id='ref34556647.37264943' class='cite'>Stock <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).&nbsp;</p>
<p>Brine shrimp (<em>Artemia</em>) are the only animals that thrive at sodium concentrations above 4 M. Salt excretion is powered by the Na<sup>+</sup>,K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase (NKA), a heterodimeric (alpha/beta) pump. Artemia express several NKA catalytic alpha-subunit subtypes. High-salinity adaptation increases the abundance of alpha2(KK), an isoform that contains two lysines (Lys308 and Lys758 in transmembrane segments TM4 and TM5, respectively) at positions where canonical NKAs have asparagines (i.e., <em>Xenopus</em> alpha1's Asn333 and Asn785). <em>Artemia</em> express two salinity-independent canonical alpha subunits (alpha1(NN) and alpha3(NN)), as well as two beta variants, in addition to the salinity-controlled alpha2(KK). These beta subunits permitt the heterologous expression of the alpha2(KK) pump and determination of its CryoEM structure in a closed, ion-free conformation, showing Lys758 residing within the ion-binding cavity. Characterization of the function of alpha2(KK) pumps was achieved (<span id='ref34557817.38079564' class='cite'>Artigas <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). The double substitution N333K/N785K confers alpha2(KK)-like characteristics to <em>Xenopus</em> alpha1, and mutant cycle analysis revealed energetic coupling between these two residues, illustrating how alpha2(KK)'s Lys308 helps to maintain high affinity for external K<sup>+</sup> when Lys758 occupies an ion-binding site. By measuring uptake under voltage clamp of the K<sup>+</sup>-congener <sup>86</sup>Rb<sup>+</sup>, double-lysine-substituted pumps transport 2Na<sup>+</sup> and 1 K<sup>+</sup> per catalytic cycle. These results show how the two lysines contribute to generating a pump with reduced stoichiometry, allowing <em>Artemia</em> to maintain steeper Na<sup>+</sup> gradients in hypersaline environments (<span id='ref34557817.38079564' class='cite'>Artigas <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).</p>
<p>The generalized reactions for P-type ATPases are:</p>
<p align='center'>nMe<sub>1</sub> (out) + mMe<sub>2</sub> (in) + ATP &rarr;  nMe<sub>1</sub> (in) + mMe<sub>2</sub> (out) + ADP + P<sub>i</sub>.</p>

[Epub:  Ahead of Print]<BR><HR></TD></TR><TR><TD COLSPAN=5><B>Examples:</B><P></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD>TC#</TD><TD>Name</TD><TD>Organismal Type</TD><TD>Example</TD></TR><TR><TD COLSPAN=5><B>Examples:</B><P></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD>TC#</TD><TD>Name</TD><TD>Organismal Type</TD><TD>Example</TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.1.1"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.1.1&tcname=">3.A.3.1.1</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Na<sup>+</sup>-, K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase (Na<sup>+</sup> efflux; K<sup>+</sup> uptake).&nbsp; Kinetic alterations due to a missense mutation in the alpha2 subunit cause familial hemiplegic migraine type 2 (<span id="ref34551241.15308625" class="cite">Segall <em>et al.</em> 2004</span>). Mutation in the &gamma;-subunit causes renal hypomagnesemia, associated with hypocalciurea (<a>Cairo <em>et al</em>., 2008</a>). The Na/K-ATPase is an important signal transducer that not only interacts and regulates protein kinases, but also functions as a scaffold (<a>Li and Xie, 2009</a>). Capsazepine, a synthetic vanilloid, converts the Na, K-ATPase to a Na-ATPase (<a>Mahmmoud, 2008a</a>). There are alternative &alpha;- and &beta;-subunits, &alpha;<sub>1</sub>, &alpha;<sub>2</sub>,... &beta;<sub>1</sub>, &beta;<sub>2,</sub>... in muscle which form &alpha;<sub>1</sub>&beta;<sub>1</sub>, &alpha;<sub>1</sub>&beta;<sub>2</sub>, &alpha;<sub>2</sub>&beta;<sub>1</sub> and &alpha;<sub>2</sub>&beta;<sub>2</sub>, heterodimers, each with differing Na<sup>+</sup> affinities (4-13mM) (<a>Kristensen and Juel, 2010</a>). &alpha;<sub>3</sub> and &beta;<sub>3</sub> isoforms have also been identified. The &gamma;-subunit is the same as TC# 1.A.27.2.1. <a>Poulsen <em>et al.</em> (2010)</a> have proposed a second ion conduction pathway in the C-terminal part of the ATPase. The two C-terminal tyrosines stabilize the occluded Na/K pump conformations containing Na or K ions (<a>Vedovato and Gadsby, 2010</a>). Na<sup>+</sup>, K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase mutations causing familial hemiplegic migraines type 2 (FHM2) inhibit phosphorylation (<a>Schack <em>et al.</em>, 2012</a>). Salt, the vascular Na<sup>+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup>&nbsp;ATPase and the endogenous glycosides, ouabain and marinobufagenin, play roles in systemic hypertension (<a>Hauck and Frishman, 2012</a>).&nbsp;Protein kinase A (PKA) phosphorylation of Ser936 (in the intracellular loop between
transmembrane segments M8 and M9) opens an intracellular C-terminal water pathway leading to the third Na<sup>+</sup>-binding site (<a>Poulsen <em>et al.</em>, 2012</a>). PKA-mediated phosphorylation regulates activity in vivo. Ser-938 is located (<span>Einholm <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>). E960 on the Na<sup>+</sup>-K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase and F28 on phospholemman (PLM) are critical for phospholemman (PLM) inhibition, but there is at least one additional site that is important for tethering PLM to the ATPase. Mutations in the Na<sup>+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase &alpha;3 subunit gene (ATP1A3) cause rapid-onset dystonia-parkinsonism, a rare movement disorder characterized by sudden onset of dystonic spasms and slow movements <a>(</a><span>Doğanli <em>et al.</em> 2013</span>).&nbsp; The 3-d strcuture of the Na<sup>+</sup>-bound Na<sup>+</sup>,K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase at 4.3&nbsp;&Aring; resolution reveals the positions of the three Na<sup>+</sup> ions (<span>Nyblom <em>et al.</em> 2013</span>).&nbsp; Mutations cause adrenal hypertension (<span>Kopec <em>et al.</em> 2014</span>) as well as alternating hemiplegia of childhood (AHC) and rapid-onset dystonia-
parkinsonism (RDP) (<span>Rosewich <em>et al.</em> 2014</span>).&nbsp; Differences in the structures of the ouabain-, digonxin- and bufalin-bound enzyme have been reported (<span>Laursen <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>).&nbsp; ATPase inhibitors have been shown to be effective anti-cancer agents (<span>Alevizopoulos <em>et al.</em> 2014</span>). Cys45 in the &beta;-subunit can be glutathionylated, regulating the activity of the enzyme (<span>Garcia <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>). ATP1A2 mutations play a role in migraine headaches (<span>Friedrich <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>). The beta2 subunit is
essential for motor physiology in mammals, and in contrast to beta1 and beta3, beta2
stabilizes the Na<sup>+</sup>-occluded E1P state relative to the outward-open E2P state (<span>Hilbers <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>). Numerous transcription factors, hormones, growth factors, lipids, and 
extracellular stimuli as well as epigenetic signals modulate the transcription of Na,K-ATPase 
subunits (<span>Li and Langhans 2015</span>). <span>Čechov&aacute; <em>et al.</em> 2016</span> have identified two cytoplasmic pathways along the pairs of TMSs, TMS3/TMS7 or TM6S/TMS9 that allow hydration of the cation binding sites or transport of cations from/to the bulk medium. Dissipation of the transmembrane gradient of K<sup>+</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup> due to ouabain inhibition increases Ptgs2 and Nr4a1 transcription by increasing Ca<sup>2+</sup> influx through L-type Ca<sup>2+</sup> channels that, in turn, leads to CaMKII-mediated phosphorylation of CREB and calcineurin-mediated dephosphorylation of NFAT, respectively (<span>Smolyaninova <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>). ZMay play a role in the development of gastric adenocarcinomas (<span>Wang <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>). Mutations F785L and T618M give rise to familial rapid onset dystonia parkonsonism by distinct mechanisms (<span>Rodacker <em>et al.</em> 2006</span>). Reacts with methylglyoxal to inhibit its activity (<span>Svrckova <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>).&nbsp; Accumulation of beta-amyloid (Abeta) at the early stages of Alzheimer's disease is accompanied by reduction of Na,K-ATPase functional activity.&nbsp;<span>Petrushanko <em>et al.</em> 2016</span> showed that monomeric Abeta(1-42) forms a tight (Kd of 3 m&mu;M), enthalpy-driven equimolar complex with alpha1beta1 Na,K-ATPase. Complex formation results in dose-dependent inhibition of the enzyme hydrolytic activity. The binding site of Abeta(1-42) is localized in the "gap" between the &alpha;- and &beta;-subunits of Na,K-ATPase, disrupting the enzyme functionality by preventing the subunits from shifting towards each other. Interaction of Na,K-ATPase with exogenous Abeta(1-42) leads to a pronounced decrease of the enzyme transport and hydrolytic activities and Src-kinase activation in neuroblastoma cells SH-SY5Y. This interaction allows regulation of Na,K-ATPase activity by short-term increases in the Abeta(1-42) level (<span>Petrushanko <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>). Two distinct phospholipids bind to two distinct sites on the ATPase, affecting activity and stability (<span>Habeck <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>). Five cysteinyl residues (C452, C456, C457, C577, and C656) serve as the cisplatin binding sites on the cytoplasmic loop connecting transmembrane helices 4 and 5 (<span>&Scaron;eflov&aacute; <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>). Mutations can cause F/SHM with moderate penitrance (<span id="ref34549586.28058944" class="cite"><span id="ref34549586.28058944" class="cite">Prontera <em>et al.</em> 2018</span></span>).&nbsp; Arginine substitution of a cysteine in transmembrane helix M8 converts the Na<sup>+</sup>,K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase to an electroneutral pump similar to the gastric H<sup>+</sup>,K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase (<span id="ref34549747.28028214" class="cite">Holm <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>). Early onset life-threatening epilepsy can be associated with ATP1A3 gene variants (<span id="ref34549897.30392841" class="cite">Ishihara <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>), and loss of Na/K pump function is the common feature of mutants that induce hyperaldosteronism (<span id="ref34550798.30811176" class="cite">Meyer <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). Three Na<sup>+</sup> sites are defined in the Na<sup>+</sup>-bound crystal structure of the Na<sup>+</sup>, K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase. Sites I and II overlap with two K<sup>+</sup> sites in the K<sup>+</sup>-bound structure, whereas site III is unique and Na<sup>+</sup> specific. A glutamine in transmembrane helix M8 (Q925) appears from the crystal structures to coordinate Na<sup>+</sup> at site III, but does not contribute to K<sup>+</sup> coordination at sites I and II.&nbsp;<span id="ref34552478.31527711" class="cite">Nielsen <em>et al.</em> 2019</span> addressed the functional role of Q925 in the various conformational states of this-ATPase by examining the mutants Q925A/G/E/N/L/I/Y both enzymatically and electrophysiologically, thereby revealing their Na<sup>+</sup> and K<sup>+</sup> binding properties. Q925 substitutions had minor effects on Na<sup>+</sup> binding from the intracellular side of the membrane, but mutations Q925A and Q925G increased the apparent Na<sup>+</sup> affinity, but caused dramatic reductions of the binding of K<sup>+</sup> as well as Na<sup>+</sup> from the extracellular side of the membrane. Thus, an interaction between sites III and I and a possible gating function of Q925 in the release of Na<sup>+</sup> at the extracellular side are supported (<span id="ref34552478.31527711" class="cite">Nielsen <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). The alpha2 Na<sup>+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase isoform mediates LPS-induced neuroinflammation (<span id="ref34552488.32843655" class="cite">Leite <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). Apical periodontitis induces changes on oxidative stress parameters and increases Na<sup>+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase activity in adult rats (<span id="ref34552509.32847752" class="cite">Barcelos <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). Familial hemiplegic migraine type 2 can be due to a missense mutation (L425H) in ATP1A2 (<span id="ref34552867.33711927" class="cite">Antonaci <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). Mutations in ATP1A3 and FXYD genes (TC# 1.A.27) can cause childhood-onset schizophrenia (<span id="ref34553149.29895895" class="cite">Chaumette <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). The ATPase plays a role in cell adhesion, motility, and migration of cancer cells (<span id="ref34553641.33713729" class="cite">Silva <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). TMS2 moves outward as Na<sup>+</sup> is deoccluded from the E1 conformation (<span id="ref34553661.33597302" class="cite">Young and Artigas 2021</span>). Kinetic properties and crystal structures of the Na<sup>+</sup>,K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase in complex with cardiotonic steroids (CTS) has revealed differences between CTS subfamilies.&nbsp;<span id="ref34553830.33502848" class="cite">Ladefoged <em>et al.</em> 2021</span> found beneficial effects of K<sup>+</sup> on bufadienolide binding in contrast to the well-known antagonism between K<sup>+</sup> and cardenolides. Bufadienolide binding is affected by (i) electrostatic attraction of the lactone ring by a cation and (ii) the ability of a cation to stabilize and "shape" the site constituted by transmembrane helices of the alpha-subunit (&alpha;M1-6). The latter effect was due to varying coordination patterns involving amino acid residues from helix bundles &alpha;M1-4 and &alpha;M5-10. Substituents on the steroid core of a bufadienolide add to and modify the cation effects (<span id="ref34553830.33502848" class="cite">Ladefoged <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). The Na<sup>+</sup>-K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase functions in the developing hippocampus (<span id="ref34554262.33206576" class="cite">Shao <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). CryoEM analyses of the Na<sup>+</sup>,K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase in the two E2P states with and without cardiotonic steroids has revealed mechanistic details (<span id="ref34554824.35380894" class="cite">Kanai <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). A conserved ion-binding site tyrosine plays a role in ion selectivity of the Na<sup>+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup> pump (<span id="ref34554895.35657726" class="cite">Spontarelli <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). Essential roles of the Na+K+-ATPase in ischemic pathology provide a platform for the improvement in clinical research on ischemic stroke (<span id="ref34554901.34839371" class="cite">Zhu <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). In intact live cells, the regulatory complex is composed of two alpha subunits associated with two beta subunits, decorated with two PLM regulatory subunits. Docking and molecular dynamics (MD) simulations generated a structural model of the complex. alpha-alpha subunit interactions support conformational coupling of the catalytic subunits, which may enhance the NKA turnover rate (<span id="ref34554946.35339486" class="cite">Seflova <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). There is a phenotypic continuum of <em>ATP1A3</em>-related disorders (<span id="ref34555394.36192182" class="cite">Vezyroglou <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). The Na<sup>+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase is central to the pathogenesis of neurological diseases such as alternating hemiplegia of childhood. This ATPase has 3 distinct ion binding sites I-III. Binding of Na<sup>+</sup> at each site in the human alpha3 Na<sup>+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase can be resolved using extracellular Na<sup>+</sup>-mediated transient currents. When the ATPase is constrained to bind and release only Na<sup>+</sup>, three kinetic components: fast, medium, and slow, can be isolated, depending on the voltage step direction and the occlusion (or deocclusion) of each of the 3 Na<sup>+</sup>s. Patient-derived mutations of residues which coordinate Na<sup>+</sup> at site III exclusively impact the slow component, demonstrating that site III is crucial for deocclusion and release of the first Na<sup>+</sup> into the extracellular milieu (<span id="ref34555608.36304555" class="cite">Moreno <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). The opening of ion channels in cardiomyocytes is regulated by the surface electric double layer of the cell membrane as revealed by studies with digoxin (<span id="ref34555639.36456624" class="cite">Zhou <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). The effects of H<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> on cys residues and other targets in the Na,K-ATPase have been discussed (<span id="ref34555697.36331672" class="cite">Chkadua <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). The influence of the Na<sup>+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase on neurodegenerative diseases has been reviewed (<span id="ref34555765.36552839" class="cite">Zhang <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). The presence of two oppositely directed transmembrane ion gradients (for Na<sup>+</sup> and K<sup>+</sup>) is important for robust stabilization of cellular volume in human erythrocytes (<span id="ref34555790.36542609" class="cite">Ataullakhanov <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>).&nbsp; This ATPase plays important roles in neurodegenerative diseases (<span id="ref34555765.36552839" class="cite">Zhang <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). <span style="font-weight: normal;">Na/K-ATPase signaling tonically inhibits sodium reabsorption in the renal proximal tubule (<span id="ref34556021.36856735" class="cite">Mukherji <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). </span>Hypoxic stress-dependent regulation of Na,K-ATPase in ischemic heart disease has been reviewed (<span id="ref34556590.37175562" class="cite">Baloglu 2023</span>).&nbsp; Chronic testosterone deficiency increases late inward sodium current and promotes triggered activity in ventricular myocytes from aging male mice (<span id="ref34556849.37389950" class="cite">Banga <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). Three dynamic lipid interaction sites in the plasma membrane Na<sup>+</sup>,K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase influence activity of the transporter (<span id="ref34556860.37481079" class="cite">Mahato and Andersson 2023</span>). Interaction of pumps and transporters positioned at distant biological 
membranes with various forms of energy transfer between them may result in hypoxic/reperfusion injury, different kinds of muscle fatigue,
 and nerve-glia interactions (<span id="ref34556967.37468808" class="cite">Dimitrov 2023</span>). Marinobufagenin (MBG) is a member of the bufadienolide family of 
compounds, which are natural cardiac glycosides found in a variety of 
animal species, including man, which have different physiological and 
biochemical functions but have a common action on the inhibition of the Na<sup>+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase. MBG acts
 as an endogenous cardiotonic steroid, and in the last decade, its role 
as a pathogenic factor in various human diseases has emerged (<span id="ref34556983.37446363" class="cite">Carullo <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).&nbsp; A malfunctioning gene product is required for disease induction by ATP1A1 variants and that if a pathology is associated with protein-null variants, they may display low penetrance or high age of onset (<span id="ref34557215.37659504" class="cite">Spontarelli <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). The Na,K-ATPase is a murzyme, facilitating thermodynamic equilibriums at the membrane-interface (<span id="ref34557411.36502470" class="cite">Manoj <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).&nbsp; The Na<sup>+</sup>K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase is present in boar sperm HPM, and it changes during capacitation (<span id="ref34557603.38029410" class="cite">Awda <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). Clinical features of CAPOS syndrome are caused by maternal <em>ATP1A3</em> gene variation (<span id="ref34557770.38297853" class="cite">Gao <em>et al.</em> 2024</span>).Inositol hexakisphosphate kinase 1 (IP6K1), governs the degradation of Na<sup>+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase via an autoinhibitory domain of PI3K p85&alpha; (<span id="ref34557889.38350944" class="cite">Jin <em>et al.</em> 2024</span>).&nbsp; Two lysines in the transmembrane segments contribute to generate a pump with reduced stoichiometry (2 K<sup>+</sup>/1 Na<sup>+</sup>), allowing <em>Artemia</em> to maintain steeper Na<sup>+</sup> gradients in hypersaline environments (<span id="ref34557817.38079564" class="cite">Artigas <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).</p>

<p>Putative archaeal Na<sup>+</sup>, K<sup>+</sup><sup> </sup> ATPase, Mac8 (encoded with methylcobalamin: coenzyme M methyltransferase; methanol-specific, a metal chaparone protein and an electron transfer protein) (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=3.A.3#ref7508">Chan <em>et al.</em>, 2010</a>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Archaea</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Putative Na<sup>+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup> ATPase of <em>Methanosarcina acetivorans</em> (Q8THY0)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.1.11"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.1.11&tcname=">3.A.3.1.11</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p><a></a></p>
<p><!-- test--></p>
<p><a id="3.A.3.1.11"></a></p>
<p>Na<sup>+</sup>,K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase &alpha;2 subunit, ATP1a2a or ATPA2A. Deficiency causes brain ventricle dilation and embryonic motility in zebra fish<em>. </em>Is essential for skeletal and heart muscle function (<span id="ref34545672.23097043" class="cite">Doganli <em>et al.</em> 2012</span>).<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=3.A.3#ref10034"> </a></p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>ATPA2 of <em>Danio rerio</em> (Q90X34)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.1.12"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.1.12&tcname=">3.A.3.1.12</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Na<sup>+</sup>,K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase subunits &alpha; (837 aas) and&nbsp;&beta; (302 aas) of the blood fluke ().</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals (Platyhelminthes)</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Na<sup>+</sup>,K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase subunits &alpha; and &beta; of <em>Schistosoma mansoni</em><br />alpha, G4VGA0<br />beta, G4VTH6</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.1.13"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.1.13&tcname=">3.A.3.1.13</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>H<sup>+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase, ATP12A or ATP1AL1 of 1039 aas and 10 TMSs in a 2 + 2 + 6 TMS arrangement.&nbsp; It plays a role in myocardial relaxation (<span id="ref34544273.25366262" class="cite">Knez <em>et al.</em> 2014</span>), but also functions in airway surface liquid acidification which impaires airway host defenses in cells lacking or compromised for CFTR (TC# 3.A.1.202.1) (<span id="ref34544992.26823428" class="cite">Shah <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>). This non-gastric H<sup>+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup> ATPase (ATP12A) is expressed in mammalian spermatozoa (<span id="ref34554641.35162971" class="cite">Favia <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>) and may be sodium ion sensitive (<span id="ref34557303.37482134" class="cite">Abe <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). Its cryoEM structures are known, and the mechanism has been explained (<span id="ref34557303.37482134" class="cite">Abe <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>), providing insight into the molecular mechanism of the E2-E1 transition and cooperative Na<sup>+</sup>-binding in the NKA and other related cation pumps.
    .</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>ATP12A of <em>Homo sapiens</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.1.14"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.1.14&tcname=">3.A.3.1.14</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Na+/K+-ATPase of 1227 aas and 10 TMSs. Involved in cell signaling, volume regulation, and
maintenance of electrochemical gradients (<span id="ref34545134.26961431" class="cite">Morrill <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>ATPase of <em>Paramecium tetraurelia</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.1.15"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.1.15&tcname=">3.A.3.1.15</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Silkworm nerve Na<sup>+</sup>,K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase,&nbsp;&alpha;-subunit of 1009 aas and 10 TMSs (77% identical to the human ortholog). and the &beta;-subunit of 326 aas and 1 TMS (30% identical to the human homolog). This ATPase, in contrast to mamalian ATPases, has high affinity for K<sup>+</sup>, but low affinity for Na<sup>+</sup>, suggesting that the &beta;-subunit is responsible for the difference in Na<sup>+</sup> affinity (<span id="ref34549236.29047027" class="cite">Homareda <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Na<sup>+</sup>,K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase of<em> Bombyx mori </em>(domestic silkworm)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.1.16"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.1.16&tcname=">3.A.3.1.16</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>ATPase, Na+/K+ transporting subunit alpha 4, ATP1A4, of 1029 aas and 10 TMSs.  Alternatively spliced transcript variants, encoding different isoforms, have been identified. ATP1A4 is exclusively expressed in germ cells and sperm and is essential for male fertility as it interacts with signaling molecules in both raft and non-raft fractions of the sperm plasma membrane to regulate capacitation-associated signaling, hyperactivation, sperm-oocyte interactions, and activation. ATP1A4 activity and expression increase during capacitation, challenging the widely accepted dogma of sperm translational quiescence (<span id="ref34555102.35887284" class="cite">Tiwari <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>ATP1A4 of <em>Homo sapiens</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.1.2"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.1.2&tcname=">3.A.3.1.2</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>H<sup>+</sup>-, K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase (gastric; H<sup>+</sup> efflux; K<sup>+</sup> uptake). Two H<sub>3</sub>O<sup>+</sup> may be transported per ATP hydrolyzed.&nbsp; Howeve, a cryo-electron microscope structure suggests that 1 H<sup>+</sup> and 1 K<sup>+</sup> are transporter per ATP hydrolyzed, providing the energy needed to generate the one million fold H<sup>+</sup> concentration gradient effected by this enzyme (<span id="ref9653.23091039" class="cite">Abe <em>et al.</em> 2012</span>).&nbsp; The detailed mechanism has been discussed, and the roles of essential residues have been proposed (<span id="ref10603.23733700" class="cite">Shin <em>et al.</em> 2011</span>).&nbsp; A number of inhibitors of acid secretion have been identified, and these are of pharmacological importance (<span id="ref10603.23733700" class="cite">Shin <em>et al.</em> 2011</span>). The catalytic alpha subunit has ten
transmembrane segments with a cluster of intramembranal carboxylic amino acids located in the middle
of TMSs 4, 5, 6 and 8. The beta subunit has one TMS with the N terminus in the cytoplasm. The
extracellular domain of the beta subunit contains six or seven N-linked glycosylation sites.
N-glycosylation is important for enzyme assembly, maturation and sorting (<span id="ref34546307.18536934" class="cite">Shin <em>et al.</em> 2009</span>). The cryo-EM structure with bound BYK99, a high-affinity member of K<sup>+</sup>-competitive, imidazo[1,2-a]pyridine inhibitors, has been solved (<span id="ref34549476.28747707" class="cite">Abe <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Gastric H<sup>+</sup>-, K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase from <em>Homo sapiens </em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.1.3"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.1.3&tcname=">3.A.3.1.3</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>Na<SUP>+</SUP>-ATPase </TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>Marine algae </TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>Na<SUP>+</SUP>-ATPase (HANA) of <I>Heterosigma akashiwo</I></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.1.4"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.1.4&tcname=">3.A.3.1.4</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Non-gastric H<sup>+</sup>-, K<sup>+</sup>- or NH<sub>4</sub><sup>+</sup>-ATPase (<span id="ref3250.16046397" class="cite">Swarts <em>et al</em>., 2005</span>; <span id="ref5019.18032481" class="cite">Worrell <em>et al.</em>, 2008</span>)</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>H<sup>+</sup>-, K<sup>+</sup> or NH<sub>4</sub><sup>+</sup>-ATPase of <em>Rattus norvegicus</em> (P54708)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.1.5"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.1.5&tcname=">3.A.3.1.5</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>Putative spirochete Na<sup>+</sup>, K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase, Lbi6 (1046 aas) (K.
