It may be a Mn<sup>2+</sup>-exporting ATPase (by similarity).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Alveolata</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>ATPase of <em>Cryptosporidium parvum</em> (Q5CTJ9)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.10.19"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.10.19&tcname=">3.A.3.10.19</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Mn<sup>2+</sup>-exporting ATPase, ATP13A1 of 1204 aas.&nbsp; Defects cause Mn<sup>2+</sup>-dependent neurological disorders.&nbsp; Orthologous to the yeast Mn<sup>2+</sup>-ATPase, Spf1 (<span>Cohen <em>et al.</em> 2013</span>). It is present in the endoplasmic reticulum while the other P5 ATPases, A2 - A5, are in overlapping compartments of the endosomal system (<span>S&oslash;rensen <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>). It complements the yeast ER ATPase, SPF1 (TC#3.A.3.10.3) although ATP13A2 - 5 do not, and unlike these latter proteins, it seems to have 12 (rather than 10) TMSs, with the two extra ones in an N-terminal domain (<span>S&oslash;rensen <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>).  ATP13A1 (Spf1 in yeast) directly interacts with the TMSs of mitochondrial tail-anchored proteins (<span id="ref34552084.32973005" class="cite">McKenna <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). P5A-ATPase mediates the extraction of mistargeted proteins from the endoplasmic reticulum (ER). Cryo-electron microscopy structures of <em>Saccharomyces cerevisiae</em> Spf1 (TC# 3.A.3.10.3) revealed a large, membrane-accessible substrate-binding pocket that alternately faced the ER lumen and cytosol and an endogenous substrate resembling an alpha-helical TMS. Thus, the P5A-ATPase can dislocate misinserted hydrophobic helices flanked by short basic segments from the ER. TMS dislocation by the P5A-ATPase establishes an additional class of P-type ATPase substrates and may correct mistakes in protein targeting or topogenesis (<span id="ref34552084.32973005" class="cite">McKenna <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). It has been designated as a transmembrane islocase (<span id="ref34554548.34147299" class="cite">Dederer and Lemberg 2021</span>). It was initially thought to mediate manganese transport (<span id="ref10760.24392018" class="cite">Cohen <em>et al.</em> 2013</span>). However,
 it was later shown to specifically bind moderately hydrophobic TMSs with short hydrophilic lumenal domains that misinsert into
 the endoplasmic reticulum (<span id="ref34552084.32973005" class="cite">McKenna <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). The P5A-ATPase extracts mistargeted or mis-inserted TMSs from the ER membrane for protein quality control, while the P5B-ATPases mediate export of polyamines from late endo-/lysosomes into the cytosol (<span id="ref34555968.36724561" class="cite">Sim and Park 2023</span>). ATP13A1 prevents ERAD of folding-competent mislocalized and misoriented proteins (<span id="ref34557357.36283413" class="cite">McKenna <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). Thus, the P5A-ATPase ATP13A1 prevents the accumulation of mislocalized and misoriented proteins, which are eliminated by different ER-associated degradation (ERAD) pathways in mammalian cells. Without ATP13A1, mitochondrial tail-anchored proteins mislocalize to the ER through the ER membrane protein complex and are cleaved by signal peptide peptidase for ERAD. ATP13A1 also facilitates the topogenesis of a subset of proteins with an N-terminal TMS or a signal sequence that should insert into the ER membrane with a cytosolic N terminus. Without ATP13A1, such proteins accumulate in the wrong orientation and are targeted for ERAD by distinct ubiquitin ligases. Thus, ATP13A1 prevents ERAD of diverse proteins capable of proper folding (<span id="ref34557357.36283413" class="cite">McKenna <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>ATP13A1 of <em>Homo sapiens</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.10.2"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.10.2&tcname=">3.A.3.10.2</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p><a></a></p>
<p><!-- test--></p>
<p><a id="3.A.3.10.2"></a></p>
<p>Zebrafish ATP13A2 (Parkinson''s disease protein) is essential for embryonic survival (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=3.A.3#ref10035">Lopes da Fonseca <em>et al.</em> 2013</a>). A missense variant in Australian Cattle Dogs give rise to late onset neuronal ceroid lipofuscinosis (<span id="ref34549655.30956123" class="cite">Schmutz <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Fish</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>ATP13A2 of <em>Danio rerio</em> (Q7SXR0)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.10.20"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.10.20&tcname=">3.A.3.10.20</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Probable divalent cation transporting ATPase 13A4, ATP13A4, of 1196 aas and 10 TMSs. This protein had been suggested to be a Mg<sup>2+</sup> transporter, but the evidence is equivocal (<span>Sch&auml;ffers <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>). It may be a Mn<sup>2+</sup>/Ca<sup>2+</sup> exporter. This protein as well as ATP13A2 has been implicated in Parkinson's disease and autism spectrum disorder (<span>S&oslash;rensen <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>). ATPA2 - 5 are all in compartments of the endosomal system and all have 10 TMSs with overlapping functions, often in different amounts in different tissues (<span>S&oslash;rensen <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>). Dysregulation of polyamine homeostasis strongly associates with human 
diseases. ATP13A2 is mutated in juvenile-onset Parkinson's 
disease and autosomal recessive spastic paraplegia 78. It is a 
transporter that balances the polyamine concentration between the 
lysosome and the cytosol. Single-particle cryo-EM solved high-resolution
 structures of human ATP13A2 in six intermediate states, including the 
putative E2 structure for the P5 subfamily of P-type ATPases. These 
structures comprise a nearly complete conformational cycle spanning the 
polyamine transport process and capture multiple substrate binding sites
 distributed along the transmembrane regions, suggesting a potential 
polyamine transport pathway (<span id="ref34556455.37031211" class="cite">Mu <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>ATP13A4 of <em>Homo sapiens</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.10.21"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.10.21&tcname=">3.A.3.10.21</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Divalent cation transporting ATPase of 1207 aas and 9 putative TMSs, Catp-6.&nbsp; <em>C. elegans</em> has three paralogues, Catp5, Catp6 and Catp7, with overlapping tissue expression patterns and functions (<span>Zielich <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>).&nbsp;</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Catp-5 of <em>Caenorhabditis elegans</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.10.22"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.10.22&tcname=">3.A.3.10.22</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Manganese transporter of 1179 aas and 12 probable TMSs (<span id="ref34548641.14996215" class="cite">Ticconi <em>et al.</em> 2004</span>).&nbsp; <span>Mediates manganese transport into the 
endoplasmic reticulum. The ATPase activity is required for cellular 
manganese homeostasis. Plays an important role in pollen
 and root development through its impact on protein secretion and 
transport processes (<span id="ref34548642.16291648" class="cite">Jakobsen <em>et al.</em> 2005</span>). Functions together with LPR1 and LPR2 in a common 
pathway that adjusts root meristem activity to phosphate availability (<span id="ref34548643.19666499" class="cite">Ticconi <em>et al.</em> 2009</span>).</span></p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>PDR2 of <em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em> (Mouse-ear cress)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.10.23"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.10.23&tcname=">3.A.3.10.23</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Cation transporting P-type ATPase, ATP1, of 2400 aas</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>ATP1 of <em>Plasmodium falciparum</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.10.24"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.10.24&tcname=">3.A.3.10.24</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Cation transporter, ATP3, of 2393 aas.</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>ATP3 of <em>Plasmodium falciparum</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.10.25"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.10.25&tcname=">3.A.3.10.25</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Cation (Mn<sup>2+</sup>?) ATPase (ATP10) of 1918 aas</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>ATP10 of <em>Plasmodium falciparum</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.10.3"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.10.3&tcname=">3.A.3.10.3</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>The endoplasmic reticular ATPase, Spf1 or Cod1. PIt pays a role in ER Mn<sup>2+</sup> homeostasis by pumping Mn<sup>2+</sup> into the ER lumen (<span id="ref4030.12058017" class="cite">Cronin et al., 2002</span>; <span id="ref10760.24392018" class="cite">Cohen <em>et al.</em> 2013</span>).  Deletion of the gene results in ER stress and lowered Mn<sup>2+</sup> in the ER lumen (<span id="ref10760.24392018" class="cite">Cohen <em>et al.</em> 2013</span>). This SPF1-ATPase is a transmembrane helix dislocase, used to remove some mislocated TM proteins from the outer membranes of mitochondria (<span id="ref34552084.32973005" class="cite">McKenna <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). Spf1p exhibits unique structures at its N-terminus, including two putative additional transmembrane domains, and a large insertion connecting the P domain with transmembrane segment M5 (<span id="ref34552579.33507968" class="cite">Petrovich <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). The Spf1p P5A-ATPase "arm-like" domain is not essential for ATP hydrolysis but its deletion impairs autophosphorylation (<span id="ref34554202.34087682" class="cite">Grenon <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). Structures of this dismutase and Msp1 reveal how they remove mislocalized TA proteins from the ER and outer mitochondrial membranes, respectively (<span id="ref34555155.35850079" class="cite">Sinning and McDowell 2022</span>). ATP hydrolytic activity of purified Spf1p correlates with micellar lipid fluidity and is dependent on conserved residues in transmembrane helix M1. Free movement of the M1 helix represent an energetic constraint on catalysis, and this constraint is likely lost in the purified preparations, resulting in protein with intrinsic spontaneous ATP hydrolytic activity. Removal of the N-terminal part of the protein apparently removes this activity (<span id="ref34555609.36264897" class="cite">Ipsen and S&oslash;rensen 2022</span>). Inhibition of P5A-ATPases such as Spf1p could potentiate metal ion-induced ER stress and proteotoxicity (<span id="ref34555679.36328152" class="cite">Petrovich <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Fungi</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Spf1 of <em>Saccharomyces cerevisiae</em> (P39986)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.10.7"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.10.7&tcname=">3.A.3.10.7</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>This protein, ATP13A2 or P5A ATPase or ATP13A(1), was orginally designated the functionally uncharacterized P-type ATPase, FUPA13 (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=3.A.3#ref7507">Thever and Saier 2009</a>).&nbsp; It is the Parkinson''s disease (PD) gene product, PARK9 (ATP13A2), and its defect gives rise to multiple abnormalities (<span id="ref9624.22885599" class="cite">Dehay <em>et al.</em> 2012</span>).&nbsp; It is similar to the incorrectly assigned manganese exporter in yeast, Ypk1 (TC# 3.A.3.10.8), and may have the same function, but in  lysosomes, it is a polyamine (spermine/spermidine) exporter (<span id="ref34553938.31996848" class="cite">van Veen <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). Toxic levels of manganese or abnormal levels of polyamines may cause a syndrome simiilar to PD (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=3.A.3#ref8923">Chesi <em>et al.</em> 2012).</a>&nbsp; Manganese homeostasis in the nervous system has been reviewed (<span id="ref34544130.25982296" class="cite">Chen <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>).&nbsp; The progression of PD may involve the
lysosome and different autophagy pathways (<span id="ref34544330.26207393" class="cite">Gan-Or <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>).&nbsp; It exhibits an activity-independent scaffolding role in trafficking/export of intracellular cargo in response to proteotoxic stress (<span id="ref34547407.28334751" class="cite">Demirsoy <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>). Mutations cause rare early onset Parkinson's disease (<span id="ref34549028.29903538" class="cite">Suleiman <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>). ATP13A2 modulates astrocyte-mediated neuroinflammation via NLRP3 inflammasome activation, thus bringing to light a direct link between astrocyte lysosomes and neuroinflammation in the pathological model of PD (<span id="ref34550626.26848562" class="cite">Qiao <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>). ATP13A2 and its close homologs, collectively known as P5B-ATPases, are polyamine transporters in endo-/lysosomes. Cryo-EM structures of human ATP13A2 in five distinct conformational 
intermediates, which together, represent a near-complete transport cycle
 of ATP13A2, have been determined. The structural basis of the polyamine specificity was 
revealed by an endogenous polyamine molecule bound to a narrow, 
elongated cavity within the transmembrane domain. The structures show an
 atypical transport path for a water-soluble substrate, in which 
polyamines may exit within the cytosolic leaflet of the membrane (<span id="ref34553937.34715013" class="cite">Sim <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). Spermine is exported from the lysosome. The transmembrane domain
 serves as a substrate binding site, and the C-terminal domain is 
essential for protein stability and may play a regulatory role (<span id="ref34553973.34728622" class="cite">Chen <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). The carcinogenic effects of ATP13A2 in different tumors has been studied (<span id="ref34553939.33743014" class="cite">Zheng and Li 2021</span>). High-resolution cryo-EM structures of human ATP13A2 in five distinct conformational intermediates have been determined, which together, represent a near-complete transport cycle of ATP13A2. The structural basis of the polyamine specificity was revealed by an endogenous polyamine molecule bound to a narrow, elongated cavity within the transmembrane domain. The structures show an atypical transport path for a water-soluble substrate, in which polyamines may exit within the cytosolic leaflet of the membrane (<span id="ref34553937.34715013" class="cite">Sim <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). Mutations in ATP13A2 aare associated with mixed neurological presentations and iron toxicity due to nonsense-mediated decay (<span id="ref34554253.33091395" class="cite">Kırımtay <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). The importance of the protein in regulating neuronal integrity has been established, and the structural dynamics and catalytic mechanism have been proposed (<span id="ref34554386.34676749" class="cite">Mateeva <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). It is an ATPase which acts as a lysosomal polyamine exporter with high affinity for spermine, and also stimulates cellular uptake of polyamines and protects against polyamine toxicity (<span id="ref34553938.31996848" class="cite">van Veen <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>; <span id="ref34555968.36724561" class="cite">Sim and Park 2023</span>). Dysregulation of polyamine homeostasis strongly associates with human 
diseases. ATP13A2 is mutated in juvenile-onset Parkinson's 
disease and autosomal recessive spastic paraplegia 78. It is a 
transporter that balances the polyamine concentration between the 
lysosome and the cytosol. Single-particle cryo-EM solved high-resolution
 structures of human ATP13A2 in six intermediate states, including the 
putative E2 structure for the P5 subfamily of P-type ATPases. These 
structures comprise a nearly complete conformational cycle spanning the 
polyamine transport process and capture multiple substrate binding sites
 distributed along the transmembrane regions, suggesting a potential 
polyamine transport pathway (<span id="ref34556455.37031211" class="cite">Mu <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>PARK9 of <em>Homo sapiens</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.10.8"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.10.8&tcname=">3.A.3.10.8</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>This protein, P5B-ATPase, was originally designated the functionally uncharacterized P-type ATPase 14 (FUPA14) (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=3.A.3#ref7507">Thever and Saier 2009)</a>, but it has been shown to be a vacuolar ATPase, Ypk1, that functions in manganese detoxification and homeostasis (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=3.A.3#ref8923">Chesi <em>et al.</em> 2012).</a>&nbsp; It is therefore likely to catalyze export of manganese ions from the cytoplasm into the vacuole. However, it also transporter polyamines such as spermine . The 3-D structure has been determined to 3.4 &Aring;, and it revealed three separate transport cycle intermediates, including spermine-bound conformations (<span id="ref34553683.34172751" class="cite">Li <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). In the absence of cargo, Ypk9 rests in a phosphorylated conformation auto-inhibited by the N-terminus. Spermine uptake into vesicles is accomplished through an electronegative cleft lined by transmembrane segments 2, 4 and 6 (<span id="ref34553683.34172751" class="cite">Li <em>et al.</em> One has an ATP analogue, a Mg<sup>2+</sup> and two Ca<sup>2+</sup> ions in the respective binding sites (<span id="ref34551526.15229613" class="cite">Toyoshima and Mizutani 2004</span>). In this state, the ATP reorganizes the three cytoplasmic domains (A, N and P), which are widely separated without nucleotide, by directly bridging the N and P domains. The structure of the P-domain itself is altered by the binding of the ATP analogue and Mg<sup>2+</sup>. As a result, the A-domain is tilted so that one of the TMSs moves to lock the cytoplasmic gate of the transmembrane Ca<sup>2+</sup>-binding sites. This appears to be the mechanism for occluding the bound Ca<sup>2+</sup> ions, before releasing them into the lumen of the sarcoplasmic reticulum (<span id="ref34551526.15229613" class="cite">Toyoshima and Mizutani 2004</span>). Molecular dynamics simulations provided evidence for the role of the Mg<sup>2+</sup> and K<sup>+</sup> bound states in the transport mechanism (<span id="ref34544182.24760008" class="cite">Espinoza-Fonseca <em>et al.</em> 2014</span>).&nbsp; Animal SERCAs are inhibited by three short single TMS membrane proteins, phospholamban (TC# 1.A.50.1), sarcolipin (1.A.50.2) and myoregulin (1.A.50.3), and the inhibitory actions of these peptides on SERCA are counteracted by a peptide called DWORF (Dwarf ORF) (<span id="ref34544979.26816378" class="cite">Nelson <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>; <span id="ref34544980.25640239" class="cite">Anderson <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>).&nbsp; <span id="ref34547478.28467821" class="cite">Norimatsu <em>et al.</em> 2017</span> have resolved the first layer of phospholipids surrounding the 
transmembrane helices. Phospholipids follow the movements 
of associated residues, causing local distortions and changes in 
thickness of the bilayer. The entire protein tilts during 
the reaction cycle, governed primarily by a belt of Trp residues, to 
minimize energy costs accompanying the large perpendicular movements of 
the transmembrane helices. A class of Arg residues extend their side 
chains through the cytoplasm to exploit phospholipids as anchors for 
conformational switching (<span id="ref34547478.28467821" class="cite">Norimatsu <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>). The human ortholog (O14983) is 96% identical to this rabbit enzyme.&nbsp; These enzymes are inhibited by the fungal mycotoxins, cyclopiazonic acid and thapsigargin (<span id="ref34550384.8038861" class="cite">Darby <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>; <span id="ref34550385.30307768" class="cite">Houdou <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). The human ortholog is 97% identical and is of the same length. The SERCA residue Glu340 mediates interdomain communication that guides Ca<sup>2+</sup> transport (<span id="ref34552656.33229570" class="cite">Geurts <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). The SERCA Ca<sup>2+</sup>-ATPase may serve as a calcium-sensitive membrane-endoskeleton sensor in the sarcoplasmic reticulum (<span id="ref34552956.33807779" class="cite">Nakamura <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). The C-terminal proton release pathway is a functional element of SERCA which provides a mechanistic model for its operation in the catalytic cycle of the pump (<span id="ref34553019.33805255" class="cite">Espinoza-Fonseca 2021</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>SERCA of  <em>Oryctolagus cuniculus</em> (rabbit)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.2.44"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.2.44&tcname=">3.A.3.2.44</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Crayfish basolateral plasma membrane Ca<sup>2+</sup>-ATPase, PMCA, of 1190 aas (<span id="ref34546345.17446457" class="cite">Wheatly <em>et al.</em> 2007</span>). 80% identical to the human orthologue.</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>PMCA of <em>Procambarus clarkii </em>(Red swamp crayfish)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.2.45"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.2.45&tcname=">3.A.3.2.45</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>The calmodulin-sensitive plasma membrane Ca<sup>2+</sup>-ATPase (PMCA) of 1080 aas and 10 TMSs.&nbsp; It has a non-canonical calmodulin (CaM) binding domain that contains a C-terminal 1-18 motif (<span id="ref34547487.28213174" class="cite">P&eacute;rez-Gordones <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>PMCA of <em>Trypanosoma equiperdum</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.2.46"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.2.46&tcname=">3.A.3.2.46</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Ca<sup>2+</sup>-ATPase of 880 aas and 10 TMSs, Ca1.&nbsp; Key intermediates have been identified; Ca<sup>2+</sup> efflux is rate-limited by phosphoenzyme formation. The transport process involves reversible steps and an irreversible step that follows release of ADP and extracellular release of Ca<sup>2+</sup> (<span>Dyla <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Ca1 of <em>Listeria monocytogenes</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.2.47"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.2.47&tcname=">3.A.3.2.47</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Putative Ca<sup>2+</sup> P-type ATPase, TMEM94, of 1356 aas and 10 TMSs in the usual 2 + 2 + 6 TMS arrangement.&nbsp; This protein is very distantly related to all other members of the 3.A.3.2 family within the P-type ATPase superfamily, and therefore may have a different or unique function (<span id="ref34548805.30265558" class="cite">Zhang <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>TMEM94 of <em>Homo sapiens</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.2.48"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.2.48&tcname=">3.A.3.2.48</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Sarco/endoplasmic reticulum Ca<sup>2+</sup> ATPase of 1018 aas and 10 TMSs (<span id="ref34548951.29886036" class="cite">Roegner <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>SARCA of <em>Callinectes sapidus </em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.2.49"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.2.49&tcname=">3.A.3.2.49</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Sarcoplasmic reticular Calcium-ATPase of 993 aas and 10 TMSs. Elongation and contraction of scallop sarcoplasmic reticulum (SR): ATP Stabilizes Ca<sup>2+</sup>-ATPase crystalline array elongation of SR vesicles (<span id="ref34554536.35328731" class="cite">Nakamura <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Ca<sup>2+</sup>-ATPase of <em>Mizuhopecten yessoensis</em> (Yesso scallop)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.2.5"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.2.5&tcname=">3.A.3.2.5</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p><a id="3.A.3.2"></a></p>
<p><!-- test--></p>
<p><a id="3.A.3.2.5"></a></p>
<p>The Golgi Ca<sup>2+</sup>, Mn<sup>2+</sup>-ATPase, human SPCA1 or SPCA1a (secretory-pathway Ca<sup>2+</sup>-ATPases (SPCAs)), ATP2C1 or Hussy-28 (efflux) (the Hailey-Hailey disease protein) is involved in responses to Golgi stress, apoptosis and mid-gestational death (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=3.A.3#ref4541">Okunade <em>et al</em>., 2007</a>). SPCA1 transports Mn<sup>2+ </sup> from the cytosol into the Golgi lumen. Increasing Golgi Mn<sup>2+ </sup> transport increased cell viability upon Mn<sup>2+ </sup>exposure, supporting a role in the management of Mn<sup>2+</sup>-induced neurotoxicity (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=3.A.3#ref7557">Mukhopadhyay and Linstedt, 2011</a>). SPCA1 governs the stability of TMEM165 (TC# 2.A.106.2.2) in Hailey-Hailey disease (<span id="ref34552649.32335229" class="cite">Roy <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). Loss of ATP2C1 leads to impaired Notch1 signalling; ATP2C1-loss could promote a mechanism by which NOTCH1 is endocytosed and degraded (<span id="ref34556040.36789506" class="cite">Zonfrilli <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).&nbsp; Six cryo-EM structures of hSPCA1 in a series of intermediate states have revealed its near-complete conformational cycle (<span id="ref34556688.37258749" class="cite">Wu <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). With the aid of molecular dynamics simulations, these structures offer a clear structural basis for Ca<sup>2+</sup> entry and release in hSPCA1. hSPCA1 undergoes unique conformational changes during ATP binding and phosphorylation compared to other well-studied P-type II ATPases. In addition, a conformational distortion of the Ca<sup>2+</sup>-binding site induced by the separation of transmembrane helices 4L and 6 unveiling a distinct Ca<sup>2+</sup> release mechanism. A structure of the long-sought CaE2P state of P-type IIA ATPases provided insight into the Ca<sup>2+</sup> transport cycle (<span id="ref34556688.37258749" class="cite">Wu <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). Structures of human SPCA1a in the ATP and Ca<sup>2+</sup>/Mn<sup>2+</sup>-bound (E1-ATP) state and the metal-free phosphorylated (E2P) state at 3.1- to 3.3-&Aring; resolutions (<span id="ref34556891.36867705" class="cite">Chen <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). The structures revealed that Ca<sup>2+</sup> and Mn<sup>2+</sup> share the same metal ion-binding pocket with similar but notably different coordination geometries in the transmembrane domain, corresponding to the second Ca<sup>2+</sup>-binding site in sarco/endoplasmic reticulum Ca<sup>2+</sup>-ATPase (SERCA).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>hSPCA1 of <em>Homo sapiens</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.2.50"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.2.50&tcname=">3.A.3.2.50</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Plasma membrane calcium-transporting ATPase, ATP2B3, of 1220 aas and 10 TMSs. This 3ATP-driven Ca<sup>2+</sup> ion pump is involved in the maintaining basal intracellular Ca<sup>2+</sup> levels at the presynaptic terminals (<span id="ref34554882.25953895" class="cite">Cal&igrave; <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>; <span id="ref34554883.27035656" class="cite">Tauber <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>). It uses ATP as an energy source to transport cytosolic Ca<sup>2+</sup> ions across the plasma membrane to the extracellular compartment, and<br />may counter-transport protons.</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>ATP2B3 of <em>Homo sapiens</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.2.51"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.2.51&tcname=">3.A.3.2.51</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Ca<sup>2+</sup>/Na<sup>+</sup>/H<sup>+</sup>-ATPase, ATP4, putative, of 1264 aas</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>ATP4 of <em>Plasmodium falciparum</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.2.52"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.2.52&tcname=">3.A.3.2.52</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Putative Ca<sup>2+</sup> ATPase, ATP9, of 1834 aas.</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>ATP9 of <em>Plasmodium falciparum</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.2.53"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.2.53&tcname=">3.A.3.2.53</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>CtpF, a probable Ca<sup>2+</sup> ATPase of 905 aas and 10 TMSs in a 2 + 2 + 6 TMS arrangement.&nbsp; Specific targeting to the <em>Mycobacterium tuberculosis</em> P-type ATPase membrane transporter, CtpF, by antituberculous compounds obtained by structure-based design, has been demonstrated (<span id="ref34557660.38149543" class="cite">Santos <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>CtpF of <em>Mycobacterium tuberculosis</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.2.6"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.2.6&tcname=">3.A.3.2.6</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>Ca<sup>2+</sup>, Mn<sup>2+</sup>- ATPase (efflux) </TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>Fungi</TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>Pmr1 of <I>Neurospora crassa</I></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.2.7"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.2.7&tcname=">3.A.3.2.7</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p><a></a></p>
<p><!-- test--></p>
<p><a id="3.A.3.2.7"></a></p>
<p>The sarco/endoplasmic reticulum Ca<sup>2+ </sup>-ATPase, SERCA2b or ATP2A2, is encoded by the ATP2A2 gene.&nbsp; Mutatioins give rise to Darier''s disease; the spectrum of mutations have been related to patients' phenotypes (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=3.A.3#ref2564">Ahn <em>et al</em>., 2003; <span id="ref9973.20423818" class="cite">Godic <em>et al.</em> 2010</span></a>).&nbsp; SERCA1 functions as a heat generator in mitochondria of brown adipose tissue (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=3.A.3#ref3434">de Meis <em>et al</em>., 2006</a>). It normally functions as a Ca<sup>2+</sup>:H<sup>+ </sup> antiporter (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=3.A.3#ref4657">Karjalainen et al., 2007</a>).  Capsaicin converts SERCA to a Ca<sup>2+ </sup> non-transporting ATPase that generates heat, and is thus a natural drug that augments uncoupled SERCA, resulting in thermogenesis (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=3.A.3#ref5538">Mahmmoud, 2008b</a>).&nbsp;Oligomeric interactions of the N-terminus of sarcolipin with the Ca-ATPase have been documented (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=3.A.3#ref8544">Autry <em>et al.</em>, 2011</a>), and these interactions also uncouple ATP hydrolysis from Ca<sup>2+</sup> transport (<span id="ref34544421.25882845" class="cite">Sahoo <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>) resulting in thermogenesis.&nbsp; TMS 11, absent in SERCA1a and SERCA2a, functions in regulation (<span id="ref9039.22528494" class="cite">Gorski <em>et al.</em> 2012</span>). The bovine SERCA has also been crystallized (2.9 &Aring; resolution; <span id="ref9080.22387132" class="cite">Sacchetto <em>et al.</em>, 2012</span>).&nbsp; These enzymes are regulated differentially by phospholamban (PLN; 1.A.50.1.1) and sarcolipin (SLN; 1.A.50.2.1) as noted above (<span id="ref9625.23362265" class="cite">Gorski <em>et al.</em> 2013</span>).&nbsp; SERCA2 is regulated by TMEM64 (9.B.27.5.1), a 380 aa 6 TMS membrane protein of the DedA family (TC# 9.B.27) which regulates Ca<sup>2+</sup> oscillations by direct interaction with CIRCA2, modulating its activity and influencing osteoblast differentiation (<span id="ref10614.23395171" class="cite">Kim <em>et al.</em> 2013</span>).&nbsp;  Animal SERCAs are inhibited by three short single (C-terminal) TMS membrane 
proteins, phospholamban (TC# 1.A.50.1), sarcolipin (1.A.50.2) and 
myoregulin (1.A.50.3), and the inhibitory actions of these peptides on 
SERCA are counteracted by a peptide called DWORF (Dwarf ORF) (<span id="ref34544979.26816378" class="cite">Nelson <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>; <span id="ref34544980.25640239" class="cite">Anderson <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>).  Small ankyrin 1 (sAnk1; TC#8.A.28.1.2) and
sarcolipin (TC# 1.A.50.2.1) interact in their transmembrane domains to regulate SERCA (<span id="ref34546757.28487373" class="cite">Desmond <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>). Reduced SERCA function preferentially affects Wnt signaling by retaining E-cadherin in the endoplasmic reticulum and promotes apoptosis (<span id="ref34549287.30625314" class="cite">Suisse and Treisman 2019</span>). There is strong coupling between the chronological order of deprotonation, the entry of water molecules into the TM region, and the opening of the cytoplasmic gate. Deprotonation of E309 and E771 is sequential with E309 being the first to lose the proton. Deprotonation promotes the opening of the cytoplasmic gate but leads to a productive gating transition only if it occurs after the transmembrane domain has reached an intermediate conformation (<span id="ref34549303.30539761" class="cite">Rui <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>). Coordination at cation binding sites I and II is optimized for Ca<sup>2+</sup> and to a lesser extent for Mg<sup>2+</sup> and K<sup>+</sup> (<span id="ref34549721.30807147" class="cite">Sun <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). Methyglyoxal reacts with and inhibits SERCA (<span id="ref34549736.30015246" class="cite">Zizkova <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>). The phospholamban pentamer alters the function of SERCA (<a class="reflink" href="../../search/result.php?tc=1.A.50#ref34550896">Glaves <em>et al.</em> 2019</a>). TMS11 followed by the luminal tail is inhibitory.&nbsp;<span id="ref34551736.31018135" class="cite">Inoue <em>et al.</em> 2019</span> determined the crystal structures of SERCA2b and its C-terminal splicing variant SERCA2a, both in the E1-2Ca<sup>2+</sup>-adenylylmethylenediphosphonate (AMPPCP) state. TMS11 is located adjacent to TMS10 and interacts weakly with a part of the L8/9 loop as well as the N-terminal end of TMS10, thereby inhibiting the SERCA2b catalytic cycle (<span id="ref34551736.31018135" class="cite">Inoue <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). Accordingly, mutational disruption of the interactions between TMS11 and its neighboring residues caused SERCA2b to display SERCA2a-like ATPase activity. The authors proposed that TMS11 serves as a key modulator of SERCA2b activity by fine-tuning the intramolecular interactions with other transmembrane regions. <span id="ref34552147.32675243" class="cite">Kabashima <em>et al.</em> 2020</span> revealed what ATP binding does to the Ca<sup>2+</sup> pump and how nonproductive phosphoryl transfer is prevented in the absence of Ca<sup>2+</sup>. They reported that the A-domain takes an E1 position, and the N-domain occupies exactly the same position as that in the E1.ATP.2Ca<sup>2+</sup> state relative to the P-domain. As a result, ATP is properly delivered to the phosphorylation site. Yet phosphoryl transfer never takes place without filling the two transmembrane Ca<sup>2+</sup>-binding sites. This explains what ATP binding does to SERCA, and how nonproductive phosphorylation is prevented in E2 (<span id="ref34552147.32675243" class="cite">Kabashima <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). Nonannular lipid binding is not necessary for the stability of the E2 state but may become functionally significant during the E2-to-E1 transition (<span id="ref34552480.30833659" class="cite">Espinoza-Fonseca 2019</span>). Structural changes induced by the binding of rutin arachidonate to SERCA1a may shift proton balance near the titrable residues Glu771 and Glu309 into neutral species, hence preventing the binding of calcium ions to the transmembrane binding sites and thus affecting calcium homeostasis (<span id="ref34553105.32024167" class="cite">Rodr&iacute;guez and M&aacute;jekov&aacute; 2020</span>). SERCA2a is a key protein in the Ca<sup>2+</sup> cycle during heart failure (<span id="ref34553130.31701344" class="cite">Zhihao <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). Covalent conjugates of fullerene derivatives with xanthene dyes inhibit SERCA (<span id="ref34553254.32500229" class="cite">Tatyanenko <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). Autophosphorylation of the pump with two bound Ca<sup>2+ </sup>ions triggers a large conformational change that opens a gate on the luminal side of the membrane allowing the release of the ions. In response to phosphorylation, the cytoplasmic domains are partially reconfigured into an intermediate state on the path toward the E2 state with a closed luminal gate (<span id="ref34553350.33720716" class="cite">Thirman <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). In preB cells, loss of SERCA2 leads to reduced V(D)J recombination kinetics due to diminished RAG-mediated DNA cleavage (<span id="ref34554029.34033676" class="cite">Chen <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). A series of structural changes may accompany the ordered dissociation of ADP, the A-domain rotation, and the rearrangement of the transmembrane (TM) helices. The luminal gate then opens to release Ca<sup>2+</sup> toward the SR lumen. Intermediate structures on the pathway are stabilized by transient sidechain interactions between the A- and P-domains. Lipid molecules between TM helices play a key role in the stabilization (<span id="ref34554199.34593638" class="cite">Kobayashi <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). Structural bases for the conformational and functional regulation of human SERCA2b have been reported (<span id="ref34554860.35560336" class="cite">Zhang and Inaba 2022</span>). DWARF interacts with SERCA and phospholamban (PLB), counteracting the inhibitory effect of PLB on SERCA (<span id="ref34555874.36682333" class="cite">Rustad <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). p300-mediated acetylation of SERCA2a is a critical post-translational modification that decreases 
the pump's function and contributes to cardiac impairment in heart failure (<span id="ref34556011.36834924" class="cite">Gorski <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). Therapeutic approaches targeting SERCA2 and associated proteasomes might protect against Cd<sup>2+</sup>-induced cytotoxicity and renal injury (<span id="ref34556191.36983052" class="cite">Li <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). SERCA2 phosphorylation at serine 663 is a key regulator of Ca<sup>2+</sup> homeostasis in heart diseases (<span id="ref34556629.37291092" class="cite">Gonnot <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).</p>
<pre><em>	</em></pre></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>SERCA2b of <em>Homo sapiens</em> (P16615)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.2.8"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.2.8&tcname=">3.A.3.2.8</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Ca<sup>2+</sup>-ATPase (efflux) with broad Ca<sup>2+</sup> dependence (3.2-320 &mu;m).&nbsp; Probably inhibited by cipargamin and SJ1733 (<span id=""ref34548484.29637069"" class=""cite"">Meier <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Protozoa</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>PfATPase4 of <em>Plasmodium falciparum</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.2.9"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.2.9&tcname=">3.A.3.2.9</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>Ca<sup>2+</sup>,Mn<sup>2+</sup>-ATPase, hSPCA2 (ATP2C2) (efflux). 64% identical to hSPCA1 (TC #3.A.3.2.5) but lower affinity for Ca<sup>2+</sup> and more restricted tissue distribution (brain and testis); present in the trans-Golgi network. fungi; protozoa; slime molds; archaea </TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>H<sup>+</sup>-ATPase, plasma membrane of <I>Neurospora crassa</I></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.3.10"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.3.10&tcname=">3.A.3.3.10</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Plamsa membrane proton-pumping ATPase, Pma1, of 1003 aas and 10 putative TMSs in a 2 + 2 + 6 TMS arrangement.&nbsp; The mutant is unable to germinate on the host leaf surface and is
thus blocked at the pre-penetration stage. Reduction in Pma1 activity may disturb the
electrochemical transmembrane gradient, thus leading to conidia defective in turgor pressure
generation on the leaf surface. <em>L. maculans</em> possesses a second plasma
membrane H<sup>+</sup>-ATPase-encoding gene, termed<em> pma2</em> (<span id="ref34544803.18538267" class="cite">Remy <em>et al.</em> 2008</span>).</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Pma1 of  <em>Leptosphaeria maculans</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.3.11"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.3.11&tcname=">3.A.3.3.11</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Probable H<sup>+</sup> pumping P-type ATPase of 1068 aas and 10 TMSs, PMA1 (<span id="ref34547737.16730200" class="cite">Shan <em>et al.</em> 2006</span>). PnPMA1 is differentially expressed during pathogen asexual development with a more than 10-fold increase in expression in germinated cysts, the stage at which plant infection is initiated, compared to vegetative or sporulating hyphae or motile zoospores.&nbsp; PnPMA1 contains all the catalytic domains characteristic of H<sup>+</sup>-ATPases but also has a distinct domain of approximately 155 amino acids that forms a putative cytoplasmic loop between transmembrane domains 8 and 9 (<span id="ref34547737.16730200" class="cite">Shan <em>et al.</em> 2006</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>PMA1 of <em>Phytophthora nicotianae</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.3.12"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.3.12&tcname=">3.A.3.3.12</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>ATPase-7, AHA7, of 961 aas and 10 TMSs. 73% identical to AHA2 with which it shares function.&nbsp; AHA7 is autoinhibited by a sequence present in the extracellular loop between transmembrane segments 7 and 8. Autoinhibition of pump activity is regulated by extracellular pH, suggesting negative feedback regulation of AHA7 during establishment of an H<sup>+</sup> gradient. Restriction of root hair elongation is dependent on both AHA2 and AHA7, with each having different roles in this process (<span id="ref34548782.30238999" class="cite">Hoffmann <em>et al.</em> valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.3.4"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.3.4&tcname=">3.A.3.3.4</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>Putative H<sup>+</sup>-ATPase</TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>Archaea</TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>Aha1 (MJ1226) of <I>Methanococcus jannaschii</i> </TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.3.5"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.3.5&tcname=">3.A.3.3.5</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Plasma membrane H<sup>+</sup>-ATPase, TbHA1 (912 aas) (3 isoforms are present in <em>T. brucei</em>) (<span>Luo <em>et al</em>., 2006</span>). This and another H+-ATPase, (UniProt acc # Q388Z3; 97% identical to TbHA1) have been f<span><span>ound</span><span> </span><span>to</span><span> </span><span>be</span></span><span> essential</span><span> </span><span>for</span><span> </span><span>bloodstream-form</span><span> </span><span><em><span>Trypanosoma</span><span> </span><span>brucei</span></em><span> </span></span><span>through</span><span> </span><span>a</span><span> genome-wide</span><span> </span><span>RNAi</span><span> </span><span>screen (<span>Schmidt <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>).<br /></span></p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Protozoan</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>TbHA1 of <em>Trypanosoma brucei</em> (AAP30857)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.3.6"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.3.6&tcname=">3.A.3.3.6</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Plamsa membrane H<sup>+</sup>-ATPase, Pma1 (pumps protons out of the cell to generate a membrane potential and regulate cytosolic pH) (<span id="ref3715.16928681" class="cite">Liu <em>et al</em>., 2006</span>; <span id="ref6875.19916929" class="cite">Petrov, 2009</span>). TMSs 4,5,6 and 8 comprise the H<sup>+</sup> pathway where essential and important residues have been identified (<span id="ref7459.21156155" class="cite">Miranda <em>et al.</em>, 2010</span>). Residues in the loop between TMSs 5 and 6 play roles in
protein stability, function, and insertion (<span id="ref34544554.25754037" class="cite">Petrov 2015</span>).&nbsp; Pma1 interacts with the plamsa membrane Cch1/Mid1 (1.A.1.11.10) to regulate its activity by influencing the membrane potential (<span id="ref34546248.27935186" class="cite">Cho <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>).&nbsp; Asp739 and Arg811 are important residues for the biogenesis and function of the enzyme as H<sup>+</sup> transport determinants (<span id="ref34546591.28320286" class="cite">Petrov 2017</span>). Pma1, is a P3A-type ATPase and the primary protein component of the membrane compartment of Pma1 (MCP). Like other plasma membrane H<sup>+</sup>-ATPases, Pma1 assembles and functions as a hexamer, a property unique to this subfamily of P-type ATPases. It has been unclear how Pma1 organizes the yeast membrane into MCP microdomains, or why it is that Pma1 needs to assemble into a hexamer to establish the membrane electrochemical proton gradient.&nbsp;<span id="ref34554217.34750373" class="cite">Zhao <em>et al.</em> 2021</span> reported a high-resolution cryo-EM study of native Pma1 hexamers embedded in endogenous lipids. The Pma1 hexamer encircles a liquid-crystalline membrane domain composed of 57 ordered lipid molecules. The Pma1-encircled lipid patch structure likely serves as the building block of the MCP. At pH 7.4, the carboxyl-terminal regulatory &alpha;-helix binds to the phosphorylation domains of two neighboring Pma1 subunits, locking the hexamer in the autoinhibited state. The regulatory helix becomes disordered at lower pH, leading to activation of the Pma1 hexamer. The activation process is accompanied by a 6.7 A downward shift and a 40 degrees rotation of transmembrane helices 1 and 2 that line the proton translocation path. The conformational changes enabled the authors to propose a detailed mechanism for ATP-hydrolysis-driven proton pumping across the plasma membrane (<span id="ref34554217.34750373" class="cite">Zhao <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). An ER-accumulated mutant of yeast Pma1 causes membrane-related stress to induce the unfolded protein response (<span id="ref34556601.37209563" class="cite">Phuong <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Yeast</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>H<sup>+</sup>-ATPase of <em>Saccharomyces cerevisiae</em> (P05030)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.3.7"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.3.7&tcname=">3.A.3.3.7</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Plasma membrane H<sup>+</sup> ATPase, PMA1 or AHA1 of 949 aas ad 10 TMSs in a 2 + 2 + 6 TMS arrangement. Three isoforms, AHA1, 2 &amp; 3, exhibit different kinetic properties (<span id="ref4028.8300635" class="cite">Palmgren and Christensen, 1994</span>). Both the N- and C-termini are directly involved in controlling the pump activity (<span id="ref6900.20068040" class="cite">Ekberg <em>et al.</em>, 2010</span>). Methyl jasmonate elicits stomatal closure in many plant species including <em>A. thaliana</em>, and stomatal closure is
accompanied by large ion fluxes across the plasma membrane.&nbsp; These events appear to be mediated by AHA1 (<span id="ref34545478.26088926" class="cite">Yan <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>).&nbsp;It  is involved in root nutrient uptake, epidermal stomatal opening, phloem sucrose loading and unloading, and hypocotyl cell elongation (<span id="ref34554203.34607207" class="cite">Ding <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). Auxin activates two distinct, antagonistically acting signalling pathways that converge on rapid regulation of apoplastic pH, a causative determinant of growth. Cell surface-based TRANSMEMBRANE KINASE1 (TMK1) interacts with and mediates phosphorylation and activation of plasma membrane H<sup>+</sup>-ATPases for apoplast acidification, while intracellular canonical auxin signalling promotes net cellular H<sup>+</sup> influx, causing apoplast alkalinization (<span id="ref34554418.34707283" class="cite">Li <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). Hydrogen sulfide improves salt tolerance through persulfidation of PMA1 in <em>Arabidopsis </em>(<span id="ref34556445.37179518" class="cite">Ma <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). H<sub>2</sub>S promotes the binding of PMA1 to the transcriptional growth-regulating factor 4 (GRF4) through persulfidation, and then activating PMA, thus improving the salt tolerance of <em>Arabidopsis</em> (<span id="ref34556445.37179518" class="cite">Ma <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Plants</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>AHA1 of <em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em><br /> (P20649)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.3.8"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.3.8&tcname=">3.A.3.3.8</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Plasma membrane H<sup>+</sup> ATPase, AHA6 (binds 14-3-3 proteins induced by phosphorylation of Thr948, causing activation; preferentially expressed in pollen; <span id="ref6835.16607029" class="cite">Bock <em>et al.</em>, 2006</span>) (82% identical to 3.A.3.3.7).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Plants</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>AHA6 of <em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em> (Q9SH76)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.3.9"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.3.9&tcname=">3.A.3.3.9</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Proton pumping ATPase, AHA2.&nbsp; 94% identical to AHA1 (3.A.3.3.7); generates the plasma membrane pmf.&nbsp; Cation-binding pockets have been identified (<span id="ref8993.21098259" class="cite">Ekberg <em>et al.</em> 2010</span>).&nbsp; The pump has been reconstituted into "nanodiscs" in a functionally monomeric form (<span id="ref10525.23836891" class="cite">Justesen <em>et al.</em> 2013</span>).&nbsp; Regulated at the post-translation level by cis-acting auto-inhibitory domains, which can be relieved by <span class="highlight">protein</span> kinase-mediated phosphorylation or binding of specific lipid species such as lysophospholipids (<span id="ref34544168.25971968" class="cite">Wielandt <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>).&nbsp; Pumping is stochastically interrupted by long-lived (~100 seconds) 
inactive or leaky states. Allosteric regulation by pH gradients 
modulates the switch between these states but not the pumping or leakage
 rates (<span id="ref34545189.27013734" class="cite">Veshaguri <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>).&nbsp; They dynamics of the pump have been examined (<span id="ref34545329.25274016" class="cite">Guerra and Bondar 2015</span>). AHA2 drives root cell expansion (<span id="ref34548782.30238999" class="cite">Hoffmann <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>). This protein is 81% identical to the barley (<em>Hordeum vulgare</em>) HA1 of 956 aas and 10 TMSs. Plasma membrane H<sup>+</sup>-ATPase (HA1 and HA2) activity and/or expression is important for regulating the activity of K<sup>+</sup> transporters and channels under drought stress conditions (<span id="ref34550901.31443572" class="cite">Cai <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). Herbivore exposure enhances <em>A. nanus</em> tolerance to salt stress by activating the jasmonic acid-signalling pathway, increasing plasma membrane H<sup>+</sup>-ATPase activity, promoting cytosolic Ca<sup>2+</sup> accumulation, and then restricting K<sup>+</sup> leakage and reducing Na<sup>+</sup> accumulation in the cytosol (<span id="ref34552448.31674033" class="cite">Chen <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>).&nbsp; Anionic phospholipids stimulate the proton pumping activity of AHA2 (<span id="ref34557210.37685912" class="cite">Paweletz <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Plants</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Proton pumping ATPase of <em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD COLSPAN=5><B>Examples:</B><P></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD>TC#</TD><TD>Name</TD><TD>Organismal Type</TD><TD>Example</TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.30.1"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.30.1&tcname=">3.A.3.30.1</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Functionally uncharacterized P-type ATPase family 30 (FUPA30) (4 proteins from &alpha;-, &beta;- and &delta;-proteobacteria; However, there may be additional TMSs. It is a high affinity, slow turnover, heavy metal transporting ATPase CtpC 
(Rv3270), which is required for virulence. It controls 
the Mn<sup>2+</sup> cytoplasmic quota and is involved in the uploading of Mn<sup>2+</sup> into secreted metalloproteins (<a class="reflink" href="../../search/result.php?tc=8.A.201#ref34555285">Padilla-Benavides <em>et al.</em> 2013</a>). It shows a preference for Mn<sup>2+</sup>, but Zn<sup>2+</sup>, Co<sup>2+</sup> and Cu<sup>2+</sup> can act as alternative substrates although at slower turnover rates (<a class="reflink" href="../../search/result.php?tc=8.A.201#ref34555285">Padilla-Benavides <em>et al.</em> 2013</a>). It mediates resistance to zinc poisoning.&nbsp;<span id="ref34555283.35961955" class="cite">Boudehen <em>et al.</em> 2022</span> showed that zinc resistance also depends on a chaperone-like protein, PacL1 (Rv3269; TC# 8.A.201.1.1). PacL1 contains an N-terminal TMS, a cytoplasmic region with glutamine/alanine repeats and a C-terminal metal-binding motif (MBM). PacL1 binds Zn<sup>2+</sup>, but the MBM is required only at high zinc concentrations. PacL1 co-localizes with CtpC in dynamic foci in the mycobacterial plasma membrane, and the two proteins form high molecular weight complexes. Foci formation does not require flotillin or the PacL1 MBM. However, deletion of the PacL1 Glu/Ala repeats leads to loss of CtpC and sensitivity to zinc. Genes <em>pacL1</em> and <em>ctpC</em> appear to be in the same operon, and homologous gene pairs are found in the genomes of other bacteria. PacL1 colocalizes and functions redundantly with other PacL orthologs in <em>M. tuberculosis</em>. Thus, PacL proteins may act as scaffolds that assemble P-ATPase-containing metal efflux platforms mediating bacterial resistance to metal poisoning (<span id="ref34555283.35961955" class="cite">Boudehen <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Actinobacteria</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>CtpC of <em>Mycobacterium tuberculosis</em> copper (Cu<sup>2+</sup>) transporting ATPase, Ccc2</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Yeast</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Ccc2 of <em>Schizosaccharomyces pombe</em> (O59666)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.5.3"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.5.3&tcname=">3.A.3.5.3</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Cu<sup>+</sup>-, Ag<sup>+</sup>-ATPase (efflux from the cytosol into the secretory pathway) (<span id="ref3046.15634671" class="cite">Barnes <em>et al</em>., 2005</span>); ATP7B (Wilson's disease protein, &alpha;-chain) is continuously expressed in Purkinje neurons.  It delivers Cu<sup>+</sup> to the ferroxidase, ceruloplasmin, in liver and may also transport Fe<sup>2+</sup> (<span id="ref3376.15733935" class="cite">Takeda <em>et al</em>., 2005</span>).&nbsp;Critical roles for the COOH terminus of ATP7B in protein stability, trans-Golgi network retention, copper sensing, and retrograde trafficking have been reported (<span id="ref8786.21454443" class="cite">Braiterman <em>et al.</em> 2011</span>).&nbsp; Modeling suggests that Cu<sup>+</sup>-binding sites HMBDs 5 and 6 are most important for function (<span id="ref9503.23029640" class="cite">Gourdon <em>et al.</em> 2012</span>).&nbsp; ATP7B loads Cu<sup>+</sup> into newly synthesized cupro-enzymes in the trans-Golgi network and exports excess
copper out of cells by trafficking from the Golgi to the plasma membrane.&nbsp; Mutations causing disease can affect activity, stability or trafficking (<span id="ref11102.24706876" class="cite">Braiterman <em>et al.</em> 2014</span>).&nbsp; Cisplatin is a poor substrate relative to Cu<sup>+</sup>with a Km of 1 m&mu;M, and copper and cisplatin compete with each other (<span id="ref34545821.17978167" class="cite">Safaei <em>et al.</em> 2008</span>). Veratridine can bind to a site at the mouth of the channel pore in the human cardiac sodium channel, NaV1.5 (<span id="ref34554490.35216338" class="cite">Gulsevin <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). ATP7A/B contains a P-type ATPase core consisting of a membrane transport domain and three cytoplasmic domains, the A, P, and N domains, and a unique amino terminus comprising six consecutive metal-binding domains.&nbsp;<span id="ref34554567.35245129" class="cite">Bitter <em>et al.</em> 2022</span> presented a cryo-EM structure of frog ATP7B in a copper-free state. Interacting with both the A and P domains, the metal-binding domains are poised to exert copper-dependent regulation of ATP hydrolysis coupled to transmembrane copper transport. A ring of negatively charged residues lines the cytoplasmic copper entrance that is presumably gated by a conserved basic residue sitting at the center. Within the membrane, a network of copper-coordinating ligands delineates a stepwise copper transport pathway. Copper binding leads to increased dynamics in the regulatory N-terminal domain of ATP7B (<span id="ref34555389.36070320" class="cite">Or&auml;dd <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). P-type ATPases follow an alternating access mechanism, with inward-facing E1 and outward-facing E2 conformations.&nbsp;<span id="ref34555468.36045128" class="cite">Salustros <em>et al.</em> 2022</span> presented structures that reach 2.7 &Aring; resolution of a copper-specific P1B-ATPase in an E1 conformation, with complementing data and analyses. A domain arrangement that generates space for interaction with ion-donating chaperones, suggests direct Cu<sup>+</sup> transfer to the transmembrane core. A methionine serves a key role by assisting the release of the chaperone-bound ion and forming a cargo entry site together with the cysteines of the CPC signature motif. <span id="ref34556566.37074913" class="cite">Yang <em>et al.</em> 2023 </span>presented cryo-EM structures of human ATP7B in the E1 state in the apo, the putative copper-bound, and the putative cisplatin-bound forms. In ATP7B, the N-terminal sixth metal-binding domain (MBD6) binds at the cytosolic copperentry site of the transmembrane domain (TMD), facilitating the delivery of copper from the MBD6 to the TMD. The sulfur-containing residues in the TMD of ATP7B mark the copper transport pathway. By comparing structures of the E1 state of human ATP7B with the E2-P(i) state of frog ATP7B, an ATP-driving copper transport model was proposed (<span id="ref34556566.37074913" class="cite">Yang <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).&nbsp; A single ATP7 gene is present in non-chordate animals while it is divided into ATP7A and ATP7B in chordates (<span id="ref34557373.37597707" class="cite">Fodor <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Eukaryotes</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Cu<sup>+</sup>-ATPase, ATP7B, of <em>Homo sapiens</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.5.30"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.5.30&tcname=">3.A.3.5.30</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p><a></a></p>
<p><!-- test--></p>
<p><a id="3.A.3.5.30"></a></p>
<p>Copper (Cu<sup>+</sup>) exporting P-ATPase, CopA (3-D structure known to 3.2&nbsp;&Aring;; PDB# 3RFU; <span id="ref7542.21716286" class="cite">Gourdon <em>et al.</em> 2011</span>).&nbsp; The internal surface of the ATPase interacts with the copper chaparone, CopZ (<span id="ref9499.23184962" class="cite">Padilla-Benavides <em>et al.</em> 2012</span>).&nbsp; A sulfur-lined metal transport pathway has been identified (<span id="ref34544012.25956886" class="cite">Mattle <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>).&nbsp; Cu<sup>+</sup> is bound at a high-affinity transmembrane-binding site in
trigonal-planar coordination with the Cys residues of the conserved CPC motif of transmembrane
segment 4 (C382 and C384) and the conserved Methionine residue of transmembrane segment 6 (M717 of the
MXXXS motif). These residues are also essential for transport (<span id="ref34544012.25956886" class="cite">Mattle <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Bacteria</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>CopA of <em>Legionella pneumophila </em>(<span style="text-decoration: none;">Q5X2N1</span>)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.5.31"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.5.31&tcname=">3.A.3.5.31</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Mycobacterial copper transporter, MctB (<span id="ref8071.21205886" class="cite">Wolschendorf <em>et al.</em>, 2011</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Bacteria</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>MctB of <em>Mycobacterium abscessus</em>&nbsp;(B1MHH7)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.5.32"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.5.32&tcname=">3.A.3.5.32</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Copper-transporting ATPase RAN1 or HMP7 (EC 3.6.3.4) (Protein HEAVY METAL ATPASE 7) (Protein RESPONSIVE TO ANTAGONIST 1). Receptors involved in ethylene signaling can acquire their copper load by different routes and adopt the metal ion from the plasma membrane either by sequential transfer from soluble chaperones of the ATX1-family via the ER-bound copper-transporting ATPase RAN1 or by direct transfer from the soluble chaperones (<span id="ref34550946.31985325" class="cite">Hoppen and Groth 2020</span>).
