DmATP7 is the sole <em>Drosophila melanogaster</em> ortholog of the human MNK and WND copper transporters. A regulatory element drives expression in all neuronal tissues examined and demonstrates copper-inducible, Mtf-1-dependent expression in the larval midgut. Thus, an important functional role for copper transport in neuronal tissues is implied. Regulation of DmATP7 expression is not used to limit copper absorption under toxic copper conditions. The protein localizes to the basolateral membrane of DmATP7 expressing midgut cells, supporting a role in export of copper from midgut cells (<span id="ref34546833.18321764" class="cite">Burke <em>et al.</em> 2008</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>ATP7 of <em>Drosophila melanogaster </em>(Fruit fly)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.5.38"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.5.38&tcname=">3.A.3.5.38</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Cuprous ion (Cu<sup>+</sup>) exporter, CopB, of 785 aas and 8 TMSs in a 4 + 2 + 2 arrangement. The copper-transporting P1B-ATPases have been divided traditionally into two subfamilies, the P1B-1-ATPases or CopAs and the P1B-3-ATPases or CopBs. CopAs selectively export Cu<sup>+</sup> whereas previous studies have suggested that CopBs are specific for Cu<sup>2+</sup> export. Biochemical and spectroscopic characterization of <em>Sphaerobacter thermophilus </em>CopB (StCopB) showed that, while it does bind Cu<sup>2+</sup>, the binding site is not in the transmembrane domain (<span id=""ref34547808.29440418"" class=""cite"">Purohit <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>).&nbsp; StCopB exhibits metal-stimulated ATPase activity in response to Cu<sup>+</sup>, but not Cu<sup>2+</sup>, indicating that it is actually a Cu<sup>+</sup> transporter. Cu<sup>+</sup> is coordinated by four sulfur ligands derived from conserved cysteine and methionine residues. The histidine-rich N-terminal region is required for maximal activity, but is inhibitory in the presence of divalent metal ions. P1B-1- and P1B-3-ATPases may therefore all transport Cu<sup>+</sup> (<span id=""ref34547808.29440418"" class=""cite"">Purohit <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>CopB of <em>Sphaerobacter thermophilus</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.5.39"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.5.39&tcname=">3.A.3.5.39</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Cu<sup>+</sup>, Zn<sup>2+</sup>, Cd<sup>2+</sup> exporting ATPase of 815 aas and 8 TMSs, CueA. Has two N-terminal metal binding domains that are essential for resistance to these three metal ions (<span id=""ref34548059.27725778"" class=""cite"">Liang <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>CueA of <em>Bradyrhizobium liaoningense</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.5.4"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.5.4&tcname=">3.A.3.5.4</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>Ag<sup>+</sup>-ATPase (efflux) </TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>Bacteria </TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>Ag<sup>+</sup>-ATPase, SilP of <I>Salmonella typhimurium</I></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.5.40"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.5.40&tcname=">3.A.3.5.40</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Copper-exporting P-type ATPase of 742 aas and 8 TMSs (<span id="ref34550522.26013484" class="cite">Singh <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>CopA of <em>Streptococcus mutans</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.5.41"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.5.41&tcname=">3.A.3.5.41</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Cuprous ion (Cu<sup>+</sup>) exporter, CtpA (does not export Co<sup>2+</sup>, Mn<sup>2+</sup>, Ni<sup>2+</sup>, Zn<sup>2+</sup> or Cu<sup>2+</sup>).&nbsp; K<sub>m</sub> for Cu<sup>+</sup> = 0.05 &mu;M (<span id="ref34550826.25967101" class="cite">Le&oacute;n-Torres <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>).&nbsp;</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Cu<sup>+</sup> ATPase of <em>Mycobacterium tuberculosis</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.5.42"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.5.42&tcname=">3.A.3.5.42</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Copper (Cu<sup>+</sup>) exporting ATPase of 1254 aas and 8 TMSs, CrpA. While the <em>A. flavus</em> protein has not been characterized at the date of this entry, the <em>A. fumigatus</em> ortholog has been (<span id="ref34555996.36835119" class="cite">Werner <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). Cellular responses used by macrophages to counteract fungal infection is the accumulation of high phagolysosomal Cu levels to destroy ingested pathogens. <em>A. fumigatus</em> responds by activating high expression levels of <em>crpA</em>, which encodes a CrpA that actively transports excess Cu<sup>+</sup> from the cytoplasm to the extracellular environment (<span id="ref34555996.36835119" class="cite">Werner <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). Deletion of CrpA fungal-unique amino acids 1-211, containing two N-terminal Cu-binding sites, moderately increased Cu-sensitivity but did not affect expression or localization to the ER and cell surface. Replacement of CrpA fungal-unique amino acids 542-556, consisting of an intracellular loop between the second and third TMSs resulted in ER retention of the protein and strongly increased Cu-sensitivity (<span id="ref34555996.36835119" class="cite">Werner <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>CrpA of <em>Aspergillus flavus</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.5.43"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.5.43&tcname=">3.A.3.5.43</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Cu<sup>+</sup> and or Cu<sup>2+</sup> exporting ATPase, CuTP, with 2568 aas and 8 TMSs in a 2 + 2 + 2 + 2 TMS arrangement (<span id="ref34555289.35811673" class="cite">Wunderlich 2022</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>CuTP of <em>Plasmodium falciparum</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.5.5"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.5.5&tcname=">3.A.3.5.5</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Cu<sup>+</sup>, Ag<sup>+</sup>-ATPase (efflux) (<span id="ref2398.12351646" class="cite">Fan and Rosen, 2002</span>).&nbsp; There are two metal binding domains (MBDs). The distal N-terminal MBD1 possesses a function analogous to the metallochaperones of
related prokaryotic copper resistance systems and is involved in copper transfer to the
membrane-integral ion binding sites of CopA. In contrast, the proximal domain MBD2 has a regulatory
role by suppressing the catalytic activity of CopA in the absence of copper (<span id="ref34543990.25899340" class="cite">Drees <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>).&nbsp;<span style="caret-color: #000000; font-size: 13.52px; font-style: normal; font-variant-caps: normal; font-weight: normal; letter-spacing: normal; text-align: start; text-indent: 0px; text-transform: none; white-space: normal; word-spacing: 0px; -moz-text-size-adjust: auto; -webkit-text-stroke-width: 0px; text-decoration: none; display: inline !important; float: none; font-family: arial, helvetica, clean, sans-serif; color: #000000;">The functions of Me<sup>2+</sup> exporters are often supported by chaperone proteins, which scavenge the metal ions from the cytoplasm. A</span><span style="caret-color: #000000; font-size: 13.52px; font-style: normal; font-variant-caps: normal; font-weight: normal; letter-spacing: normal; text-align: start; text-indent: 0px; text-transform: none; white-space: normal; word-spacing: 0px; -moz-text-size-adjust: auto; -webkit-text-stroke-width: 0px; text-decoration: none; display: inline !important; float: none; font-family: arial, helvetica, clean, sans-serif; color: #000000;"> CopA chaperone is expressed in <em>E.&nbsp;coli</em> from the same gene that encodes the transporter (<span id="ref34549186.28107647" class="cite">Meydan <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>). Some ribosomes translating <em>copA</em> undergo programmed frameshifting, terminate translation in the -1 frame, and generate the 70 aa-long polypeptide CopA(Z), which helps cells survive toxic&nbsp;</span><span style="caret-color: #000000; font-size: 13.52px; font-style: normal; font-variant-caps: normal; font-weight: normal; letter-spacing: normal; text-align: start; text-indent: 0px; text-transform: none; white-space: normal; word-spacing: 0px; -moz-text-size-adjust: auto; -webkit-text-stroke-width: 0px; text-decoration: none; font-family: arial, helvetica, clean, sans-serif; color: #000000;">copper</span><span style="caret-color: #000000; font-size: 13.52px; font-style: normal; font-variant-caps: normal; font-weight: normal; letter-spacing: normal; text-align: start; text-indent: 0px; text-transform: none; white-space: normal; word-spacing: 0px; -moz-text-size-adjust: auto; -webkit-text-stroke-width: 0px; text-decoration: none; display: inline !important; float: none; font-family: arial, helvetica, clean, sans-serif; color: #000000;">&nbsp;concentrations. The high efficiency of frameshifting is achieved by the combined stimulatory action of a&nbsp;"slippery" sequence, an mRNA pseudoknot, and the CopA nascent chain. Similar mRNA elements are not only found in the <em>copA</em> genes of other bacteria but are also present in ATP7B, the human homolog of <em>copA,</em> and direct ribosomal frameshifting in&nbsp;vivo (<span id="ref34549186.28107647" class="cite">Meydan <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>). </span>Cu(i) (Cu<sup>+</sup>) pumps, of which CopA is an example, are primary-active electrogenic uniporters. The Cu<sup>+</sup> translocation cycle does not require proton counter-transport, resulting in electrogenic generation of a transmembrane potential upon translocation of one Cu<sup>+</sup> per ATP hydrolysis in the catalytic cycle (<span id="ref34553818.32469032" class="cite">Abeyrathna <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Bacteria</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>CopA of <em>E. coli</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.5.6"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.5.6&tcname=">3.A.3.5.6</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Cu<sup>+</sup>-ATPase, ATP7A (MNK or Mc1) (efflux from the cytosol into the secretory pathway) (Menkes disease protein, &alpha;-chain) (<span>T&uuml;mer 2013</span>). It plays a role in systemic copper absorption in the gut and copper reabsorption in the kidney. In nonpolarized cells, the metal binding sites in the amino-terminal domain of MNK are required for copper-regulated trafficking from the Golgi to the plasma membrane (<span id="ref34551436.15269005" class="cite">Greenough <em>et al.</em> 2004</span>). It is expressed in Purkinje cells early in development and later in Bergmann glia.  In melanocytes, it delivers Cu<sup>2+</sup> to tyrosinase (<a>Barnes <em>et al</em>., 2005</a>). ATP7A has dual functions: 1) it incorporates copper into copper-dependent enzymes; and 2) it maintains intracellular copper levels by removing excess copper from the cytosol. To accomplish both functions, the protein traffics between different cellular locations, depending on copper levels (<a>Bertini and Rosato, 2008</a>).&nbsp;The lumenal loop Met672-Pro707 of ATP7A binds metals and facilitates copper release from the intramembrane sites (<a>Barry <em>et al.</em>, 2011</a>).&nbsp; Modeling suggests that Cu<sup>+</sup>-binding sites HMBDs 5 and 6 are most important for function (<a>Gourdon <em>et al.</em> 2012</a>).&nbsp; In addition to X-linked recessive
Menkes disease, mutations cause occipital horn syndrome and adult-onset distal motor neuropathy (<span>Yi and Kaler 2014</span>). p97/VCP interacts with ATP7A playing a role in motor neuron degeneration (<span>Yi and Kaler 2018</span>). 55 different mutations were located around the six copper binding sites and the ATP binding site. 76.7% of the mothers were carriers. Approximately half of the male siblings of patients with MNK were diagnosed with MNK (<span id="ref34549783.30809870" class="cite">Fujisawa <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). It may play a role in melanosome (melanocyte) function (<span id="ref34553298.32333855" class="cite">Wiriyasermkul <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>ATP7A of <em>Homo sapiens</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.5.7"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.5.7&tcname=">3.A.3.5.7</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Cu<sup>+</sup>-Ag<sup>+</sup>-ATPase (efflux), CopA of 804 aas. Exhibits maximal activity at 75˚C (Cattoni et al., 2007). The 3-D structure of the ATP-binding domain has been solved (2HC8_A) (functions with the Cu<sup>+</sup> chaperone, CopZ; 130aas) (<span id="ref5110.18417453" class="cite">Gonz&aacute;lez-Guerrero and Arg&uuml;ello, 2008</span>). This protein has both N- and C- terminal metal binding domains (MBDs). The N-MBD exhibits a conserved ferredoxin-like fold, binds metals to CXXC, and regulates turnover. The C-MBD interacts with the ATP-binding (ATPB) domain and the actuator (A) domain (<span id="ref7299.20602459" class="cite">Agarwal <em>et al.</em>, 2010</span>). Cysteine is a non-essential activator of CopA, interacting with the cytoplasmic side of the enzyme in the E1 form (<span id="ref34546751.17064659" class="cite">Yang <em>et al.</em> 2007</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Euryarchaea</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>CopAZ of <em>Archaeoglobus fulgidus</em>: <br /> CopA (PaeS) (O29777) <br /> CopZ (2HU9_A; O29901)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.5.8"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.5.8&tcname=">3.A.3.5.8</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Cu<sup>+</sup> transporting ATPase (intracellular, in the trans-Golgi membrane), Ccc2</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Yeast</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Ccc2 of <em>Candida albicans</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.5.9"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.5.9&tcname=">3.A.3.5.9</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>Cu<sup>+</sup> transporting (copper detoxification) ATPase, Crp1</TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>Yeast</TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>Crp1 of <I>Candida albicans</i></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD COLSPAN=5><B>Examples:</B><P></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD>TC#</TD><TD>Name</TD><TD>Organismal Type</TD><TD>Example</TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.6.1"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.6.1&tcname=">3.A.3.6.1</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Zn<sup>2+</sup>-, Cd<sup>2+</sup>-, Pb<sup>2+</sup>-ATPase (efflux).&nbsp; Expression of the <em>zntA</em> gene is inducible by all three metal ions, with Cd<sup>2+</sup> being the most potent, mediated by the MerR-like regulator, ZntR (<span id="ref34544640.26296876" class="cite">Chaoprasid <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>). <em>zntA</em> and <em>zntR</em> mutants 
were highly sensitive to CdCl<sub>2</sub> and ZnCl<sub>2</sub>, and less sensitive to CoCl<sub>2</sub>. Inactivation of <em>zntA</em> increased the accumulation of 
intracellular cadmium and zinc and conferred hyper-resistance to H<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>. 
Thus, ZntA and its regulator, ZntR, are important 
for controlling zinc homeostasis and cadmium and cobalt detoxification. 
The loss of either the <em>zntA</em> or <em>zntR</em> gene did not affect the virulence of<em> A. tumefaciens </em>in<em> Nicotiana benthamiana</em> (<span id="ref34544640.26296876" class="cite">Chaoprasid <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>ZntA of <em>Agrobacterium tumefaciens</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.6.26"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.6.26&tcname=">3.A.3.6.26</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Cadmium/zinc resistance efflux pump, CadA of 910 aas and 8 TMSs (<span id="ref34545284.24607711" class="cite">Maynaud <em>et al.</em> 2014</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>CadA of <em>Mesorhizobium metallidurans</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.6.27"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.6.27&tcname=">3.A.3.6.27</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Transition metal efflux ATPase of 829 aas and 6 TMSs, CzcP.&nbsp; Exports Zn<sup>2+</sup>, Cd<sup>2+</sup> and Co<sup>2+</sup> efficiently (<span id="ref34545286.19602147" class="cite">Scherer and Nies 2009</span>). Mediates resistance to Pb<sup>2+</sup>, Cd<sup>2+</sup> and Zn<sup>2+</sup>.&nbsp; Lead resistance is facilitated by the phosphatase, PbrB, possibly by allowing complexation of the Pb<sup>2+ </sup>by phosphate in the periplasm (<span id="ref34544371.19737357" class="cite">Hynninen <em>et al.</em> 2009</span>). <sup><br /></sup></p>

(Q9M3H5)</TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.6.7"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.6.7&tcname=">3.A.3.6.7</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>The Zn<sup>2+</sup> (and Cd<sup>2+</sup>)-ATPase, HMA2.  HMA2 maintains metal homeostasis and has a long C-terminal sequence rich in Cys and His residues that binds Zn<sup>2+</sup>, Kd&asymp;16 nM and regulates activity (<span id="ref3552.16973620" class="cite">Eren <em>et al</em>., 2006</span>). HMA2.1 from switchgrass (<em>Panicum virgatum</em> L.) enhances cadmium tolerance in <em>Arabidopsis thaliana </em>(<span id="ref34556050.36834955" class="cite">Zang <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).<em><br /></em></p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Plants</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>HMA2 of <em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em> (Q9SZW4)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.6.8"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.6.8&tcname=">3.A.3.6.8</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>The Cd<sup>2+</sup> resistance ATPase, CadA (<span id="ref3786.16835223" class="cite">Wu <em>et al</em>., 2006</span>)</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Bacteria</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>CadA of <em>Listeria monocytogenes</em> (Q60048)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.6.9"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.6.9&tcname=">3.A.3.6.9</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>The Zn<sup>2+</sup> uptake ATPase, ZosA (YkvW) (<span id="ref4191.12180919" class="cite">Gaballa and Helmann, 2002</span>)</TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>Bacteria</TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>ZosA of <i>Bacillus subtilis</i> (O31688)</TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD COLSPAN=5><B>Examples:</B><P></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD>TC#</TD><TD>Name</TD><TD>Organismal Type</TD><TD>Example</TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.7.1"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.7.1&tcname=">3.A.3.7.1</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase (uptake), KdpFABC. (KdpA is homologous to other K<sup>+</sup> transporters such as KcsA (1.A.1.1.1), KtrB (2.A.38.4.2 and 2.A.38.4.3), and HKT (2.A.38.3.1 and 2.A.38.3.2); KdpB is homologous to P-ATPase &alpha;-subunits; KdpC and KdpF may facilitate complex assembly and stabilize the complex (<span id="ref5495.17710642" class="cite">Bramkamp <em>et al.</em>, 2007</span>; <span id="ref3736.16645309" class="cite">Haupt <em>et al</em>., 2005</span>; <span id="ref4650.18058005" class="cite">Greie and Altendorf, 2007</span>; <span id="ref7957.21711450" class="cite">Irzik <em>et al.</em>, 2011</span>).  The KdpFABC acts as a functional and structural dimer with the two KdpB subunits in direct contact, but the enzyme can dissociate to the monomer (<span id="ref5029.18298081" class="cite">Heitkamp <em>et al</em>., 2008</span>).  KdpF is part of and stabilizes the KdpABC complex (<span id="ref5030.10608856" class="cite">Gassel <em>et al</em>., 1999</span>).&nbsp; Transcription of the <em>kdp</em> operon is activated by the KdpDE sensor kinase/response regulator pair, and unphosphorylated IIA<sup>Ntr</sup> of the PTS (TC# 4.A) binds KdpD to stimulate its activity, thereby enhancing <em>kdp</em> operon expression (<span id="ref10691.19400808" class="cite">L&uuml;ttmann <em>et al.</em> 2009</span>, <span id="ref34544302.26159077" class="cite">L&uuml;ttmann <em>et al.</em> 2015).</span> Transcriptional regulation of the<em> Pseudomonas putida</em> <em>kdpFABC</em> operon by the KdpDE sensor kinase/response regulator by direct interaction of IIA<sup>Ntr</sup> of the PTS with KdpD has also been studied (<span id="ref34544303.26159078" class="cite">Wolf <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>). The 2.9 &Aring; X-ray structure of the complete <em>Escherichia coli </em>KdpFABC complex with a potassium ion within the selectivity filter of KdpA and a water molecule at a canonical cation site in the transmembrane domain of KdpB has been solved (<span id="ref34546985.28636601" class="cite">Huang <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>). The structure reveals two structural elements that appear to mediate the coupling between these two subunits: a protein-embedded tunnel runs between these potassium and water sites, and a helix controlling the cytoplasmic gate of KdpA is linked to the phosphorylation domain of KdpB. A mechanism that repurposes protein channel architecture for active transport across biomembranes was proposed (<span id="ref34546985.28636601" class="cite">Huang <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>). The cytoplasmic C-terminal domain of KdpD functions as a K<sup>+</sup> sensor (<span id="ref34549784.16484207" class="cite">Rothenb&uuml;cher <em>et al.</em> 2006</span>). Serine phosphorylated KdpB is trapped in a conformation where the ion-binding site is hydrated via an intracellular pathway between TMSs M1 and M2 which opens in response to the rearrangement of cytoplasmic domains, resulting from phosphorylation (<span id="ref34553117.33951450" class="cite">Dubey <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). This causes pump inhibition in the presence of high K<sup>+</sup> resulting in ATP conservation.</p>

</pre>
</pre></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Bacteria; proteobacteria</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>KdpABCF of <em>E. coli</em><br /> KdpA (P03959) <br /> KdpB (P03960) <br /> KdpC (P03961) <br /> KdpF (P36937)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.7.2"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.7.2&tcname=">3.A.3.7.2</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>High affinity potassium uptake ATPase, KdpABC.&nbsp; Regulated by direct interaction of the IIA<sup>Ntr</sup> protein with the sensor kinase/response regulator, KdpDE (<span id="ref10079.22340847" class="cite">Prell <em>et al.</em> 2012</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Proteobacteria</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>KdpABC of <em>Rhizobium leguminosarum</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.7.3"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.7.3&tcname=">3.A.3.7.3</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Potassium transporter, KdpABC, with 3 subunits:&nbsp; KdpA, B2HPR5, 552 aas and 10 TMSs in a 2 + 2 + 2 + 2 + 2 arrangement; KdpB ATPase, B2HRP6, 693 aas and 7&nbsp; TMSs in a 2 + 2 + 3 TMS arrangement, and KdpC, D2HRP7, 296 aas with one N-terminal TMS and possibly one C-terminal TMS.&nbsp; A <em>kdpA</em> null mutantion reduced the fraction of persisters after exposure to rifampicin (<span id="ref34550840.31913766" class="cite">Liu <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). <em>kdpA</em> encodes a transmembrane protein that is part of the Kdp-ATPase, an ATP-dependent high-affinity potassium (K<sup>+</sup>) transport system. <em>kdpA</em> expression is induced under low K<sup>+</sup> conditions and is required for pH homeostasis and growth in media with low concentrations of K<sup>+</sup>. Inactivation of the Kdp system caused hyperpolarization of the membrane potential, increased the proton motive force (PMF) and elevated levels of intracellular ATP. The KdpA mutant phenotype could be complemented with a functional <em>kdpA</em> gene or supplementation with high K<sup>+</sup> concentrations. Thus, the Kdp system is required for ATP homeostasis and persister formation. ATP-mediated regulation of persister formation may be a general mechanism in bacteria, and suggest that K<sup>+</sup> transporters may play a role in the regulation of ATP levels and persistence (<span id="ref34550840.31913766" class="cite">Liu <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>KdpABC of <em>Mycobacterium marinum</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD COLSPAN=5><B>Examples:</B><P></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD>TC#</TD><TD>Name</TD><TD>Organismal Type</TD><TD>Example</TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.8.1"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.8.1&tcname=">3.A.3.8.1</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Golgi Aminophospholipid (phosphatidyl serine and phosphatidyl ethanolamine) translocase (flipping from the exofacial to the cytosolic leaflet of membranes to generate phospholipid asymmetry), required for vesicle-mediated protein transport from the Golgi and endosomes. The system has been reconstituted after purification in proteoliposomes. It flips phosphatidyl serine but not phosphatidylcholine or sphinogomyelin (<span id="ref6801.19805341" class="cite">Zhou and Graham, 2009</span>).&nbsp; A unified mechanism of flipping for ABC and P-type ATPases has been proposed (<span id="ref11087.24746984" class="cite">L&oacute;pez-Marqu&eacute;s <em>et al.</em> 2014</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>ATPase II of <em>Bos taurus</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.8.10"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.8.10&tcname=">3.A.3.8.10</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Lipid flippase, Apt1 (involved in stress tolerance and virulence). Deletion of Apt1 causes (1) altered actin distribution, (2) increased sensitivity to stress conditions (oxidative and nitrosative stress) and to trafficking inhibitors, such as brefeldin A and monensin, a reduction in exported acid phosphatase activity, and (3) hypersensitivity to the antifungal drugs amphotericin B, fluconazole, and cinnamycin (<span id="ref.19949048" class="cite">Hu and Kronstad, 2010</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Yeast</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Apt1 of <em>Cryptococcus neoformans </em>(Q5KP96)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.8.11"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.8.11&tcname=">3.A.3.8.11</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Phospholipid (e.g., cardiolipin) transporter, Atp8b1 or ATP881 which functions with CDC50A (see TC# 8.A.27.1.5).&nbsp; The structural basis of a broad lipid specificity of ATP8B1 has been determined (<span id="ref34557619.37980352" class="cite">Dieudonn&eacute; <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). Phosphatidylinositol and cardiolipin are transport substrates. A critical role of the sn-2 ester bond of glycerophospholipids is important (<span id="ref34557619.37980352" class="cite">Dieudonn&eacute; <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). A mutant version is associated with severe pneumonia in humans and mice. It binds and internalizes cardiolipin and other phospholipids from extracellular fluid via a basic residue-enriched motif. Administration of a peptide encompassing the cardiolipin binding motif of the Atp8b1 gene product in mice lessens bacterium-induced lung injury and improves survival (<span id="ref9273.20852622" class="cite">Ray <em>et al.</em>, 2010</span>).&nbsp; Mutations have been identified that give rise to progressive familial intrahepatic cholestasis (<span id="ref9900.23060447" class="cite">Stone <em>et al.</em> 2012</span>).&nbsp; This lipid flippase forms a heterodimer with CDC50A/Transmembrane protein 30A (TC# 8.A.27.1.5) and is essential for surface expressioin of the apical Na<sup>+</sup>-bile acid transporter, Slc10A2/ASBT (TC#2.A.28.1.2) (<span id="ref34543829.25239307" class="cite">van der Mark <em>et al.</em> 2014</span>; <span id="ref34556573.37277652" class="cite">Schlosser <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Atp8b1 of <em>Homo sapiens </em>(O43520)<br /><em>&nbsp;</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.8.12"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.8.12&tcname=">3.A.3.8.12</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Probable phospholipid-transporting ATPase IF (EC 3.6.3.1) (ATPase IR) (ATPase class VI type 11B). Among the ATP10 and ATP11 proteins of P4-ATPases, ATP10A, ATP10D, ATP11A, and ATP11C localize to the plasma membrane, while ATP10B and ATP11B localize to late endosomes and early/recycling endosomes, respectively. The 3-D strcutures of 6 distinct intermediates (2.6 - 3.3 &Aring; resolution) of the complex of this protein with CDC50A (TC# 8.A.27.1.5) have been solved, revealing the transport cycle for lipid flipping (<span id="ref34550546.31416931" class="cite">Hiraizumi <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). ATP-dependent phosphorylation induces a large rotational movement of the actuator domain around the phosphorylation site in the phosphorylation domain, accompanied by lateral shifts of the first and second TMSs, thereby allowing phosphatidylserine binding. Ndc1 functions in NPC assembly at the fused inner and outer nuclear membranes. A direct interaction of Ndc1's transmembrane domain with Nup120 and Nup133, members of the pore membrane coating Y-complex. An amphipathic helix in Ndc1's C-terminal domain binds highly curved liposomes. Ndc1's amphipathic motif functionally interacts with related motifs in the C-terminus of the nucleoporins Nup53 and Nup59, important for pore membrane binding and interconnecting NPC modules. The essential function of Ndc1 can be suppressed by deleting the amphipathic helix from Nup53 (<span id="ref34556568.37154843" class="cite">Amm <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). It is the only phospholipid flipping ATPase in the human red blood cell (<span id="ref34550808.30850395" class="cite">Liou <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). <span>In the cell membrane of erythrocytes, it is 
required to maintain phosphatidylserine (PS) in the inner leaflet 
preventing its exposure on the surface. This asymmetric distribution is 
critical for the survival of erythrocytes in circulation since 
externalized PS is a phagocytic signal for splenic macrophages (<span id="ref34550809.26944472" class="cite">Arashiki <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>).
 Phospholipid translocation seems also to be implicated in vesicle 
formation and in the uptake of lipid signaling molecules, and is required for B cell differentiation past the pro-B cell stage It seems to mediate PS flipping in pro-B cells and may be involved in the transport of cholestatic bile acids.&nbsp; </span>Caspase-dependent inactivation of ATP11C is essential for an apoptotic "eat me" signal, phosphatidylserine exposure, which prompts phagocytes to engulf cells.&nbsp;<span id="ref34552243.32997992" class="cite">Nakanishi <em>et al.</em> 2020</span><strong> </strong>presented six cryo-EM structures of ATP11C at 3.0-4.0 A resolution in five different states of the transport cycle. A structural comparison revealed phosphorylation-driven domain movements coupled with phospholipid binding. Three structures of phospholipid-bound states visualize phospholipid translocation accompanied by the rearrangement of transmembrane helices and an unwound portion at the occlusion site. They thus detail the basis for head group recognition and the locality of the protein-bound acyl chains in transmembrane grooves. Invariant Lys880 and the surrounding hydrogen-bond network serve as a pivot point for helix bending and precise P domain inclination, which is crucial for dephosphorylation. The structures detail key features of phospholipid translocation by ATP11C; a common basic mechanism for flippases is emerging (<span id="ref34552243.32997992" class="cite">Nakanishi <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>).</p>

The system has been reconstituted after purification in proteoliposomes. It flips phosphatidyl serine and phosphatidyl ethanolamine, but not phosphatidylcholine or sphingomyelin (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=3.A.3#ref6801">Zhou and Graham, 2009</a>).&nbsp; Drs2p (ACT3; ATP8A2) is required for phospholipid translocation across the
 Golgi membrane. It interacts with CDC50 (TC# 8.A.27) (<a class="reflink" href="../../search/result.php?tc=3.A.3#ref7695">Bryde <em>et al.</em>, 2010</a>). Activated by ArfGEF when bound to the C-terminus (<a class="reflink" href="../../search/result.php?tc=3.A.3#ref7306">Natarajan <em>et al.</em> 2009</a>).&nbsp;The
 beta-subunit, CDC50A, allows the stable expression, assembly, 
subcellular localization, and lipid transport activity of the P4-ATPase 
ATP8A2 (<a class="reflink" href="../../search/result.php?tc=3.A.3#ref8659">Coleman and Molday, 2011</a>).&nbsp;<span id="ref34550354.31243363" class="cite">Timcenko <em>et al.</em> 2019</span> described the cryo-EM structure of Drs2p-Cdc50p, It is autoinhibited by the C-terminal tail of Drs2p and activated by the lipid phosphatidylinositol-4-phosphate (PI4P). Three structures were solved that represent the complex in an autoinhibited, an intermediate and a fully activated state. The analysis revealed sites of autoinhibition and PI4P-dependent activation. A putative lipid translocation pathway involves a conserved PISL motif in TMS 4 and polar residues of TMSs 2 and 5, in particular Lys1018, in the centre of the lipid bilayer (<span id="ref34550354.31243363" class="cite">Timcenko <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). The enzymatic cycle of P-type ATPases is divided into autophosphorylation and dephosphorylation half-reactions. Unlike most other P-type ATPases, P4-ATPases transport their substrate during dephosphorylation only, i.e. the phosphorylation half-reaction is not associated with transport. To study the structural basis of the distinct mechanisms of P4-ATPases,&nbsp;<span id="ref34553953.34023399" class="cite">Timcenko <em>et al.</em> 2021</span> determined cryo-EM structures of Drs2p-Cdc50p covering multiple intermediates of the cycle. They identified several structural motifs specific to Drs2p and P4-ATPases in general that decrease movements and flexibility of domains as compared to other P-type ATPases. These motifs include the linkers that connect the transmembrane region to the actuator (A) domain, which is responsible for dephosphorylation. Mutation of Tyr380, which interacts with conserved Asp340 of the distinct DGET dephosphorylation loop of P4-ATPases, highlights a functional role of these P4-ATPase specific motifs in the A-domain. Finally, the transmembrane (TM) domain, responsible for transport, also undergoes less extensive conformational changes, which is ensured both by a longer segment connecting TM helix 4 with the phosphorylation site, and possible stabilization by the auxiliary subunit Cdc50p. Collectively these adaptions in P4-ATPases are responsible for phosphorylation becoming transport-independent (<span id="ref34553953.34023399" class="cite">Timcenko <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). The Arf activator, Gea2p (Uniprot P39993, 1459 aas), and Drs2p interact in the Golgi (<span id="ref34554296.14734650" class="cite">Chantalat <em>et al.</em> 2004</span>).</p>

Docking of a phosphatidylcholine headgroup in silico showed that the cavity can accommodate a phospholipid headgroup, likely leaving the fatty acid tails in contact with the hydrophobic portion of the lipid bilayer. HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.8.30&tcname=">3.A.3.8.30</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Aminophospholipid flippase of 2008 aas</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font NAME="3.A.3.8.31"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.8.31&tcname=">3.A.3.8.31</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Guanylate cyclase &alpha;/&beta; subunit(s) (<span id="ref34555289.35811673" class="cite">Wunderlich 2022</span>) with 4226 aas and about 22 TMSs in a possible 2 + 2 + 6 + 6 + 6 TMS arrangement. <p><!-- test--></p>
<p><a></a></p>
<p>Inwardly directed phospholipid and lysophospholipid (phosphatidylcholine, phosphatidyl serine and lysophosphoethanolamine) flippase, Dnf1 or ATP11C (functions with the &beta;-subunit, Lem3 or CDC50A (TC# 8.A.27.1.5) (<a>Elvington <em>et al</em>., 2005</a>; <a>Pomorski <em>et al</em>., 2003</a>; <a>Riekhof and Voelker, 2006</a>; <a>Riekhof <em>et al</em>., 2007</a>) Also transports the anti-neoplastic and anti-parasitic ether lipid substrates related to edelfosine (<a>Riekhof and Voelker, 2009</a>) (not required for phosphotidyl serine inwardly directed flipping (<a>Stevens <em>et al.</em> 2008</a>)). Transports diacyl phospholipids in preference to lyso (monoacyl) phospholipids (<a>Baldridge <em>et al.</em> 2013</a>).&nbsp; A conserved asparagine (N220) in the first transmembrane segment specifies glycerophospholipid binding and transport, but specific substitutions at this site allow transport of sphingomyelin (<span>Roland and Graham 2016</span>). It transports glycosphingolipids (<span id="ref34550770.30530492" class="cite">Roland <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). <span id="ref34552628.32493773" class="cite">Nakanishi <em>et al.</em> 2020</span> presented the crystal structures of a human plasma membrane flippase, the ATP11C-CDC50A complex, in a stabilized E2P conformation. The structure revealed a deep longitudinal crevice along transmembrane helices continuing from the cell surface to the phospholipid occlusion site in the middle of the membrane. The extension of the crevice on the exoplasmic side was open, and the complex was therefore in an outward-open E2P state, similar to a cryo-EM structure of the yeast flippase Drs2p-Cdc50p complex. Phosphatidylserines were in the crevice and in its extension to the extracellular side. One was close to the phosphatidylserine occlusion site as previously reported for the human ATP8A1-CDC50A complex, and the other in a cavity at the surface of the exoplasmic leaflet of the bilayer. Substitutions in either of the binding sites or along the path between them impaired ATPase and transport activities. Thus, the crevice is the conduit along which phosphatidylserine traverses the membrane (<span id="ref34552628.32493773" class="cite">Nakanishi <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Yeast</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Dnf1 of <em>Saccharomyces cerevisiae</em> (P32660)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.8.5"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.8.5&tcname=">3.A.3.8.5</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p><a></a></p>
<p><!-- test--></p>
<p><a id="3.A.3.8.5"></a></p>
<p>Inwardly directed phosphatidylcholine, phosphatidyl serine, and lysophosphoethanolamine flippase, Dnf2 (functions with the &beta;-subunit, Lem3) (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=3.A.3#ref3248">Elvington <em>et al</em>., 2005</a>; <a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=3.A.3#ref4398">Pomorski <em>et al</em>., 2003</a>; <a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=3.A.3#ref3554">Riekhof and Voelker, 2006</a>; <a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=3.A.3#ref4542">Riekhof <em>et al</em>., 2007</a>). This plasma membrane P-type ATPase (ACT4) is a phospholipid flippase that contributes to endocytosis, protein transport and all polarity (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=3.A.3#ref7309">Hua <em>et al.</em>, 2002</a>). Transports monoacyl (lyso) phospholipids much better than diacyl phospholipids, but can be mutated to transport diacyl phospholipids (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=3.A.3#ref10053">Baldridge <em>et al.</em> 2013</a>). It transports glycosphingolipids (<span id="ref34550770.30530492" class="cite">Roland <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Yeast</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Dnf2 of <em>Saccharomyces cerevisiae</em> (Q12675)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.8.6"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.8.6&tcname=">3.A.3.8.6</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>Golgi phospholipid transporting (flipping) ATPase3 (1213aas; 10TMSs). Involved in growth of roots and shoots. Uses a &#946;-ATPase3 subunit, ALIS1 (TC#8.A.27.4) (<span id="ref5189.18344284" class="cite">Paulsen <em>et al</em>., 2008</span>).</TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>Plants</TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>ATPase3/ALIS1 of <I>Arabidopsis thaliana</I> (Q9XIE6)</TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.8.7"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.8.7&tcname=">3.A.3.8.7</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>The aminophospholipid ATPase1 (ALA1) (mediate chilling tolerance; <span id="ref5534.11148289" class="cite">Gomes <em>et al</em>., 2000</span>).&nbsp; Promotes antiviral silencing (<span id="ref34546462.28123063" class="cite">Guo <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Plants</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>ALA1 of <em>Arabidopsis thaliana </em> (P98204)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.8.8"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.8.8&tcname=">3.A.3.8.8</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>The phosphatidylserine flippase in photoreceptor disc membranes, ATP8A2 (<span id="ref6753.19778899" class="cite">Coleman <em>et al.</em>, 2009</span>).&nbsp;The beta-subunit, CDC50A (TC#8.A.27.1.5), allows the stable expression, assembly, subcellular localization, and lipid transport activity of ATP8A2 (<span id="ref8659.21454556" class="cite">Coleman and Molday, 2011</span>).&nbsp; Missennse mutations in ATP8A2 are associated with cerebellar atrophy and guadrupedal locomotion (<span id="ref8955.22892528" class="cite">Emre Onat <em>et al.</em> 2012</span>). Asparagine-905 of the mammalian phospholipid flippase ATP8A2 is essential for lipid substrate-induced activation of ATP8A2 dephosphorylation (<span id="ref34549509.30760526" class="cite">Mikkelsen <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). Phosphatidylserine flipping by the P4-ATPase, ATP8A2, is electrogenic (<span id="ref34551207.31371510" class="cite">Tadini-Buoninsegni <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). Translocation of the lipid substrate toward the cytoplasmic bilayer leaflet is comparable to unzipping a zipper of salt bridges/hydrogen bonds (<span id="ref34557220.37678495" class="cite">Mogensen <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>ATP8A2 of <em>Mus musculus </em>(P98200)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="3.A.3.8.9"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=3.A.3.8.9&tcname=">3.A.3.8.9</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>The phospholipid flipping ATPase (contributes to vesicle biogenesis in the secretory and endocytic pathways).
